Global Interdependence

4.1 Natural Increase as a Component of Population Change

Key Demographic Measures

Birth Rate (BR) — the number of live births per 1,000 people per year. Also called the crude birth rate (CBR). Simple to calculate but 'crude' because it does not account for the age structure of the population — a country with a large proportion of women of reproductive age will have a high BR even if individual women are having few children.

Death Rate (DR) — the number of deaths per 1,000 people per year (crude death rate, CDR). Similarly crude — a country with an older population will have a higher DR even if healthcare is excellent (Japan's DR has risen in recent years not because health has worsened but because the population has aged).

Natural Increase Rate (NIR) — BR minus DR, usually expressed as a percentage (divide by 10). A positive NIR means the population grows through natural change alone, before migration is considered. A negative NIR (natural decrease) occurs when DR exceeds BR. Global NIR ~0.9% (2023).

Total Fertility Rate (TFR) — the average number of children a woman would have over her reproductive lifetime (ages 15–49) if current age-specific fertility rates continued. More precise than the crude BR because it controls for age structure. Replacement level TFR = 2.1 children (slightly above 2 to account for infant mortality and the slight male excess at birth). Global TFR ~2.3 (2023); ranges from ~6.8 (Niger) to ~0.78 (South Korea).

Infant Mortality Rate (IMR) — the number of deaths of children under 1 year of age per 1,000 live births per year. One of the most sensitive single indicators of development — closely tied to the quality of maternal healthcare, access to clean water and sanitation, nutritional standards, and the education level of mothers. Ranges from ~1–2 (Japan, Iceland, Finland) to ~78 (Sierra Leone). IMR is also causally linked to fertility: the 'child survival hypothesis' holds that when parents are confident children will survive, they choose to have fewer — making IMR reduction one of the most effective long-term strategies for reducing fertility rates.

Comparative data — Niger, Bangladesh, UK and Japan (approx. 2023):
Measure Niger (LIC) Bangladesh (MIC) UK (HIC) Japan (HIC)
Birth Rate (/1,000)~46~18~10~7
Death Rate (/1,000)~8~6~9~12
NIR (%)~3.8%~1.2%~0.1%~-0.5%
TFR~6.8~2.0~1.6~1.2
IMR (/1,000)~68~24~4~2
Life expectancy~62 yrs~74 yrs~81 yrs~84 yrs
% under 15~50%~26%~18%~11%
% over 65~3%~6%~19%~29%

Note: Niger's DR (~8) appears low despite being an LIC because 50%+ of the population is under 15 — few people are old enough to die of age-related disease. This illustrates why crude rates can mislead without contextual interpretation.

Factors Affecting Levels of Fertility

Fertility rates are determined by overlapping social, economic, environmental, and political factors. For exam answers, identify the most important factor for a specific country and explain the mechanism — avoid simple lists.

Social factors:

  • Female education: The single strongest predictor of fertility reduction globally. Each additional year of female education reduces TFR by approximately 0.1 children. Education delays marriage, raises the opportunity cost of childbearing, gives women greater decision-making power, and improves access to and use of contraception. In Niger, adult female literacy is ~11% — one of the world's lowest — closely associated with its world-highest TFR of ~6.8. In Bangladesh, female secondary school enrolment rose dramatically from the 1990s, driven partly by government stipend programmes for girls — strongly correlated with TFR falling from 6.0 (1980) to ~2.0 (2023).
  • Age at marriage: Early marriage (before 18) is a major driver of high fertility. In Niger, the average age of first birth is ~18 and ~76% of girls are married before 18 — the highest rate in the world. By contrast, in Japan and the UK, average age at first birth is ~30–31, dramatically compressing the reproductive period.
  • Status of women: Where women's primary social role is defined as wife and mother, fertility tends to be high. Where women have independent economic roles, social status, and legal rights, fertility falls. Japan's ultra-low TFR reflects in part a tension between rising female career aspirations and a persistent cultural expectation that mothers are primarily responsible for childcare — many Japanese women choose not to have children rather than sacrifice career progression.
  • Access to and use of contraception: Contraceptive prevalence rate (CPR) is the proportion of women of reproductive age using any contraceptive method. Niger: CPR ~6% — extremely limited access and use. UK: CPR ~84%. Bangladesh's extraordinary fertility decline was partly achieved by government-funded community health workers delivering contraception directly to rural women's homes — removing access barriers. Contraception access alone is not sufficient if social norms favour large families, but combined with education it is highly effective.
  • Religion and cultural norms: Some religious traditions actively encourage large families or prohibit contraception. Roman Catholicism (opposition to artificial contraception), conservative Islam, and some evangelical Protestant denominations all associate with higher fertility. However, the relationship is not deterministic — Catholic Italy and Portugal have TFR ~1.3, well below replacement.
  • Urbanisation: Urban dwellers consistently have lower fertility than rural dwellers in the same country. In cities, children are an economic cost (small apartments, no farm work, high education costs) rather than an economic asset. Access to contraception, healthcare, and female employment is greater. Bangladesh's rapid urbanisation (from ~8% urban in 1971 to ~40% urban in 2023) is an important driver of its fertility transition.

Economic factors:

  • Level of development (income): As countries develop, children shift from economic assets (farm labour, old-age insurance) to economic liabilities (high costs of education, childcare, housing). In subsistence agriculture (Niger), children contribute labour from an early age and support parents in old age when no pension system exists — incentivising large families. As per-capita income rises and formal pension systems develop, the economic rationale for large families weakens.
  • Cost of raising children: In Japan and the UK, the cost of housing, childcare, and education is extremely high. In Japan, university education costs are very significant; housing in Tokyo and other cities is expensive; professional childcare is unaffordable for many. These economic barriers to having children are a primary driver of sub-replacement fertility in HICs. UK TFR of ~1.6 and Japan TFR of ~1.2 both reflect this economic constraint alongside changing social values.
  • Female employment and opportunity cost: Where women have significant earning potential, the opportunity cost of leaving the workforce to have children is high. The gender pay gap for mothers ('motherhood penalty') is a well-documented economic disincentive to childbearing in HICs.
  • Economic uncertainty: Fertility tends to fall during periods of economic recession or uncertainty as couples postpone childbearing. The 2008–09 global financial crisis caused a measurable dip in TFR across Europe. Bangladesh's young workers in the garment industry — predominantly women — have delayed marriage and childbearing, contributing to fertility decline.

Environmental factors:

  • Agricultural systems and resource dependence: In labour-intensive subsistence farming systems — such as the millet and sorghum farming of the Sahel (including Niger) — large families are economically rational because children provide agricultural labour. High child mortality in harsh environments also incentivises having more children as insurance against child death. Where farming is mechanised and families are not land-dependent (UK, Japan), this driver disappears.
  • Environmental stress and insecurity: Drought, famine, and environmental degradation can both raise and lower fertility — raising it (as parents seek more children for labour and security) or lowering it (through increased mortality and disruption of family formation). In the Sahel, recurring drought conditions have not significantly reduced Niger's TFR, partly because subsistence agriculture remains the dominant livelihood.
  • Disease environment: High child mortality from infectious disease (malaria, diarrhoea, pneumonia) historically encouraged high fertility as insurance. As vaccination programmes have reduced child mortality in Niger — IMR fell from ~320 in 1960 to ~68 today — this incentive should weaken, but cultural adaptation to lower mortality lags behind, contributing to Niger's continued high TFR.

Political factors:

  • Pro-natalist policies: Governments seeking to raise fertility use financial incentives (baby bonuses, tax credits, child allowances), parental leave entitlements, subsidised childcare, and public campaigns. France offers generous family allowances and free childcare; its TFR (~1.8) is higher than most Western European countries. Hungary's Viktor Orbán government introduced a lifetime income tax exemption for mothers of four or more children. Japan's government has spent billions on pro-natalist policies (¥100,000 per child cash payments, expanded paternity leave, subsidised daycare) with minimal impact on TFR — illustrating that financial incentives alone cannot overcome structural cultural barriers.
  • Anti-natalist policies: Policies aiming to reduce fertility include China's One Child Policy (1980–2015); India's mass sterilisation campaigns (1975–77 Emergency period); Iran's post-1988 family planning programme (which reduced TFR from 6.5 to 1.8 in 15 years through voluntary contraception distribution). Bangladesh's government-funded Community Health Worker programme — deploying female health workers to deliver contraception and maternal health advice to rural households — is widely regarded as a model for voluntary fertility reduction in low-income settings.
  • Legal framework: Legal access to contraception and abortion varies enormously. The UK's Abortion Act (1967) and the introduction of the contraceptive pill (from 1961) both contributed to rapid fertility decline in the late 1960s–70s. Access to abortion is heavily restricted in many LICs and MICs, limiting women's reproductive autonomy.
  • Conflict and instability: Political instability in Niger — including multiple coups (most recently 2023) and Sahel insurgency — has severely disrupted health services, family planning programmes, and girls' education, reinforcing conditions that sustain high fertility.

Factors Affecting Levels of Mortality

Social factors:

  • Education (especially female education): Educated mothers have dramatically lower IMR — they are more likely to use healthcare services, understand nutritional needs, implement oral rehydration therapy, seek vaccination, and recognise danger signs in pregnancy and infancy. The IMR gap between Niger (~68) and Japan (~2) is directly related to the education gap for women.
  • Access to healthcare: Quality and accessibility of hospitals, clinics, doctors, midwives, and community health workers. Japan has ~13 physicians per 1,000 people and near-universal health insurance (introduced 1961) — contributing to its world-leading life expectancy of ~84 years. Niger has ~0.04 physicians per 1,000 people. Bangladesh's community health worker network has dramatically reduced maternal and child mortality even without high incomes — showing that community-level healthcare delivery can achieve mortality reductions ahead of economic development.
  • Sanitation and clean water: Waterborne diseases (cholera, typhoid, diarrhoea) are a leading cause of child mortality in LICs. The UN estimates that improved sanitation alone could prevent ~800,000 child deaths per year globally. In Bangladesh, a major factor in reducing IMR from ~145 (1980) to ~24 (2023) has been the dramatic improvement in access to safe drinking water (from tube wells) — though arsenic contamination of some groundwater sources is a continuing concern.
  • Diet and nutrition: Malnutrition — both undernutrition (insufficient calories; protein deficiency; micronutrient deficiency) and increasingly overnutrition (obesity-related disease in HICs and rapidly developing MICs) — affects mortality. In Niger, ~40% of children under five are stunted due to malnutrition, increasing their vulnerability to infectious disease. In the UK, diet-related chronic disease (heart disease, type 2 diabetes, certain cancers) is the leading cause of premature death, increasingly concentrated in lower socioeconomic groups.
  • Lifestyle factors: In HICs, lifestyle choices — smoking, alcohol consumption, physical inactivity, diet — are the primary drivers of differential mortality between social groups and between countries. Japan's low mortality is partly attributable to dietary habits (low saturated fat, high fish consumption), cultural attitudes to physical activity, and low obesity rates (~4% vs UK ~28%).
  • Social inequality: Mortality is unequally distributed within all countries. In the UK, life expectancy in the most deprived areas (e.g. Blackpool, Middlesbrough) is ~10 years lower than in the least deprived (e.g. Kensington and Chelsea, Hart in Hampshire). This 'health inequality' reflects unequal access to healthcare, housing quality, air quality, diet, employment security, and stress.

Economic factors:

  • National wealth and healthcare investment: GDP per capita is strongly correlated with life expectancy up to roughly $15,000–20,000, after which the relationship flattens (some relatively poor countries, e.g. Cuba, Sri Lanka, Bangladesh, achieve much better health outcomes than their income would predict — showing that how wealth is distributed and invested matters as much as the total). Nigeria ($2,200 GDP per capita) invests very little in public health infrastructure relative to need.
  • Poverty: Poverty directly causes poor housing (damp, overcrowded — increasing respiratory disease), inability to buy adequate food, inability to access or afford medical treatment, and stress-related disease. In Bangladesh, economic growth and poverty reduction — from ~44% living below the poverty line in 1991 to ~18% in 2022 — has been a major driver of mortality improvement.
  • Occupational hazards: Industrial and agricultural workers face occupational mortality risks. Coal mining (pneumoconiosis); agricultural pesticide exposure; construction accidents. Wealthier countries have stronger occupational health regulation.

Environmental factors:

  • Disease environment: Tropical regions face a heavier burden of vector-borne infectious disease. Malaria alone kills ~600,000 people per year, almost all in sub-Saharan Africa, and is a significant cause of mortality in Niger. Malaria particularly affects under-5 children and pregnant women — directly raising IMR and maternal mortality. The UK's temperate climate means these tropical disease burdens are absent.
  • Natural hazards: Bangladesh has one of the world's highest natural hazard exposures — cyclones (Cyclone Bhola, 1970, ~300,000 deaths; Cyclone Sidr, 2007, ~3,500 deaths), flooding (60% of Bangladesh is floodplain), and delta erosion displace millions and cause direct mortality. Investment in cyclone shelters, early warning systems, and embankments has dramatically reduced cyclone mortality over recent decades — a clear example of adaptation reducing mortality risk.
  • Air and water pollution: Air pollution kills ~7 million people/year globally (WHO). Indoor air pollution from burning solid fuels (a major issue in LICs including Niger and rural Bangladesh) is particularly deadly for women and children. Japan's stringent air quality standards and high fuel efficiency contribute to very low pollution mortality. Contaminated water sources are a primary driver of diarrhoeal disease mortality in Niger and Bangladesh.
  • Climate: Japan and the UK experience cold winters that increase cardiovascular and respiratory mortality among the elderly, particularly where homes are poorly insulated (fuel poverty is a significant UK mortality risk). Japan's 2023 heatwave killed ~33,000 people — climate-related mortality is growing globally, disproportionately affecting the elderly.

Political factors:

  • War and conflict: Armed conflict directly kills combatants and civilians, destroys health infrastructure, causes famine (by disrupting food systems), and displaces populations into refugee conditions with high disease and mortality risk. Niger's Sahel insurgency has displaced ~700,000 people and disrupted healthcare services in affected regions. Sudan's 2023 civil war has caused mass famine and is projected to kill hundreds of thousands through direct violence and hunger.
  • Government investment in health: Universal health coverage — where governments fund healthcare accessible to all regardless of income — is strongly associated with lower mortality and lower health inequality. Japan's universal insurance system (introduced 1961) and the UK's NHS (founded 1948) are credited with their excellent mortality outcomes. Bangladesh has expanded its public health system significantly, particularly its community health worker programme — achieving mortality reductions disproportionate to its income level.
  • International aid and vaccination: GAVI (Vaccine Alliance) has funded vaccination programmes across LICs — measles, polio, rotavirus, and meningococcal vaccination have reduced child mortality substantially in Niger and Bangladesh. PEPFAR (US President's Emergency Plan for AIDS Relief) has funded HIV treatment across sub-Saharan Africa, preventing millions of deaths. These programmes illustrate how international political cooperation can achieve mortality reductions in countries too poor to fund these interventions themselves.
  • Governance and corruption: Government corruption reduces the effectiveness of health spending — funds intended for healthcare are diverted, medicines go missing, health workers are paid but do not work. Nigeria (a comparative example) has an IMR of ~60 despite being a middle-income country, largely because oil revenues that could fund healthcare have been dissipated through corruption. Bangladesh and Niger both face governance challenges, but Bangladesh's more effective delivery of community health services has produced much better health outcomes than Nigeria at a comparable income level.

Population Structure and Age/Sex Diagrams

Population structure refers to the composition of a population by age and sex. It reflects the demographic history of a country — past birth rates, death rates, migration patterns, and events such as wars or epidemics — and determines future demographic trends.

The age/sex diagram (population pyramid): A back-to-back bar chart showing the proportion (or number) of males (left) and females (right) in each age cohort, typically in 5-year bands. The overall shape reveals the stage of demographic transition and the balance of the population.

How to interpret a population pyramid — a systematic approach for exam answers:

  • The base (young cohorts): A wide base indicates a high birth rate and high proportion of children. A narrow or tapering base indicates a low and/or falling birth rate.
  • The apex (elderly cohorts): A pointed apex indicates high mortality — few people survive to old age (LIC pattern). A broad, squared-off apex indicates low mortality and long life expectancy (HIC pattern).
  • The overall shape: A true 'pyramid' (wide base narrowing to apex) = high BR, high DR, rapidly growing young population. A 'barrel' or 'coffin' shape (relatively even width across age groups) = low BR, low DR, stable population. A 'constrictive' shape (narrow base, wider middle and upper) = very low BR, ageing population.
  • Bulges and indentations: Irregularities in the pyramid's outline reflect specific historical events. A bulge in middle age cohorts = a baby boom in that generation (UK post-WWII baby boom — born ~1946–64 — creates a notable bulge now in the 60–78 age range). A narrowing at a specific cohort = a period of low birth rates (UK fertility decline of the late 1970s) or heavy mortality (missing male cohorts in countries that experienced war — e.g. Russia's 20–35 age cohort shows fewer men due to excess male mortality). Migration creates bulges in working-age groups (Gulf states have enormous pyramidal bulges in the 20–45 male cohorts due to migrant workers).
  • Sex ratio differences: At birth, ~105 boys are born per 100 girls globally. Males have higher age-specific mortality, so the sex ratio gradually equalises and eventually reverses at older ages — women predominate significantly in the 75+ cohorts in HICs. In countries with son preference and sex-selective abortion (China, India), some cohorts show skewed sex ratios. In countries with high male military mortality, female excess in middle cohorts is visible.
Niger — population pyramid characteristics:

Niger's pyramid is a classic expansive or 'true pyramid' shape. The base is very wide — ~50% of the population is under 15, reflecting a TFR of ~6.8 and a BR of ~46/1,000. Each successive older cohort is significantly smaller, reflecting high age-specific mortality throughout life. The apex is very narrow — few people survive to old age (only ~3% are over 65). The pyramid tapers steeply and consistently from base to apex. This shape indicates: Stage 2 of the DTM; a rapidly growing population (doubling time ~18 years at current NIR of ~3.8%); enormous youth dependency (dependency ratio ~115); large future growth momentum even if TFR begins to fall (because the large current child cohort will itself have children). The near-symmetry of male and female sides is notable — Niger has not experienced the sex-selective migration or significant war mortality that creates asymmetry. The pyramid's shape reflects a population structure that will exert enormous pressure on Niger's food supply, education system, and employment opportunities in the coming decades.

Bangladesh — population pyramid characteristics:

Bangladesh's pyramid reflects its rapid fertility transition — from a wide-base pyramid in the 1980s to a transitional form today. The base is moderately wide but narrower than Niger — ~26% under 15, reflecting TFR falling to ~2.0. The middle cohorts (20–50) are the widest, as the large cohorts born during the high-fertility 1980s and 1990s are now in working age — this creates a potential 'demographic dividend' if the economy can absorb this large working-age cohort into productive employment. The apex is moderately pointed, reflecting improving but not yet HIC-level life expectancy (~74 years). The pyramid has an irregular transition shape: narrowing base (falling fertility), wide middle (transitional bulge), and still-pointed apex (incomplete epidemiological transition). Bangladesh also shows a slight female deficit in older age cohorts, partly reflecting historical lower investment in women's healthcare and nutrition.

UK — population pyramid characteristics:

The UK's pyramid has a 'barrel' or near-constrictive shape, reflecting Stage 4 of the DTM with low BR (~10/1,000) and low DR (~9/1,000). The base is narrow — only ~18% under 15 — reflecting sub-replacement TFR (~1.6). The most striking feature is a pronounced bulge in the 55–75 age group — the post-WWII baby boom cohort (born 1946–1964, now aged 60–78). Below this, there is a slight narrowing as the lower-fertility generations born in the 1970s–80s enter middle age, followed by a small upturn for the children of the 'echo' baby boom generation (born in the 1980s–90s). The apex is broad and squared off — 19% are over 65, reflecting life expectancy of ~81 years. There is a marked female excess in the 75+ age groups, as women's life expectancy significantly exceeds men's (though the male-female gap has been narrowing). Irregular features: net immigration has added to working-age cohorts — particularly the 25–44 range — creating slight asymmetric bulges reflecting waves of immigration (Caribbean/South Asian Commonwealth migrants from the 1950s–70s, Eastern European EU migrants post-2004, South Asian arrivals in the 2010s–20s).

Japan — population pyramid characteristics:

Japan's pyramid is a classic 'constrictive' or 'urn' shape — the hallmark of Stage 5 of the DTM. The base is very narrow: only ~11% under 15, reflecting a TFR of ~1.2 — well below replacement. Each successive younger cohort since the 1970s has been smaller than the cohort before it, creating a pronounced inward taper at the base. The widest part of the pyramid is in the 45–70 age range — the large cohorts born in Japan's post-war economic miracle era (1950s–1970s). The apex is strikingly broad — ~29% of Japan's population is over 65, the highest proportion of any country in the world. The 75+ female cohort is particularly wide, reflecting Japan's world-leading female life expectancy (~87 years). Japan shows an almost complete absence of immigration bulges in working-age cohorts — the country has historically had extremely restrictive immigration policy, making its population ageing particularly acute. The pyramid's shape clearly indicates: natural decrease (DR > BR since 2007); a rapidly ageing population; extreme elderly dependency (dependency ratio ~70, dominated by elderly rather than youth dependency); and a shrinking working-age base that must support a growing elderly apex — the core fiscal and economic challenge of Japan's demographic future.

Population Dependency, Dependency Ratio and Consequences

Dependency refers to the proportion of a population that is economically inactive and dependent on the working-age population for support. The two groups of dependants are:

  • Youth dependants — those aged 0–14, who are too young to work and require investment in childcare, education, food, and healthcare.
  • Elderly dependants — those aged 65+, who have typically left the workforce and require pensions, healthcare, and social care.

The working-age population (ages 15–64) produces the economic output and pays the taxes that fund support for both groups.

Dependency ratio = (population under 15 + population over 65) ÷ population aged 15–64 × 100

A ratio of 60 means there are 60 dependants for every 100 working-age people. A higher ratio means each worker must support more dependants. A lower ratio means more workers per dependant — potentially creating greater economic surplus.

Dependency ratios compared — Niger, Bangladesh, UK, Japan:
Country Youth dependency Elderly dependency Total dependency ratio Dominant concern
Niger~107~5~112Youth: schools, food, healthcare
Bangladesh~40~9~49Transitional — demographic dividend window
UK~28~30~58Elderly: NHS, pensions, social care
Japan~21~49~70Elderly: world's most acute ageing crisis

Consequences of high youth dependency (Niger): With a total dependency ratio of ~112, Niger has more dependants than workers. The vast majority are youth, requiring investment in schools, teachers, child healthcare, and food. Government revenue — constrained by low GDP, limited tax base, and high levels of aid dependency — cannot meet these needs: Niger has an adult literacy rate of ~35%, reflecting decades of insufficient school provision. The large youth cohort creates enormous future pressure: when today's children reach working age, they will need jobs — which Niger's economy (predominantly subsistence agriculture) is unlikely to provide in sufficient numbers. This risks a 'demographic disaster' rather than a dividend. The rapid population growth (doubling every 18 years) also compounds land pressure — average farm sizes are shrinking as land is subdivided across growing families, reducing agricultural productivity and food security.

Consequences of the demographic dividend (Bangladesh): Bangladesh's falling youth dependency and still-low elderly dependency has created a 'demographic window' — a period when the dependency ratio is low and a large working-age cohort is available. This demographic dividend has contributed to Bangladesh's rapid economic growth (~6–7% per year over the past decade): the large pool of working-age women has fuelled the garment industry ($40bn exports, ~4 million workers, ~80% female), driving poverty reduction. However, the dividend is time-limited — Bangladesh's elderly dependency will rise significantly by 2040–50 as today's large working-age cohort ages. Planning for future pensions and elderly care is a growing policy challenge.

Consequences of ageing population (UK): The UK's dependency ratio of ~58 is rising, with elderly dependency growing as the baby boom cohort ages. Key consequences: (1) NHS pressure — older people use healthcare services disproportionately; ~50% of NHS spending is on over-65s. The UK's NHS is under severe strain, with record waiting lists partly reflecting an elderly demand surge. (2) Pension costs — the state pension costs ~£110bn/year and is index-linked to inflation; as more people claim for longer, costs rise. The pension age has been raised (to 66, with 67 planned for 2028) to reduce costs. (3) Social care — care for elderly people with dementia and other conditions is funded by local authorities and individuals, creating a 'social care crisis' as demand outstrips funding. (4) Labour shortages — a shrinking working-age proportion has been partly offset by immigration (particularly from EU and Commonwealth countries), but Brexit has reduced EU inflows. (5) Political power — older voters are more likely to vote and to vote conservatively, potentially skewing political priorities toward elderly interests (pensions, healthcare) over younger ones (housing, education).

Consequences of extreme ageing (Japan): Japan's dependency ratio of ~70, dominated by elderly dependency (~49), represents perhaps the world's most acute demographic challenge. Japan spends ~35% of its national budget on social security (pensions and healthcare). The working-age population is shrinking: Japan had ~87 million working-age people in 1995; this is projected to fall to ~52 million by 2060. Labour shortages are acute across healthcare, agriculture, construction, and services. Japan's responses include: automation and robotics (the world's highest robot density in manufacturing); increasing female labour force participation (from ~55% to ~73% since 2012 under Abenomics reforms); modest increases in immigration (immigration rose to ~400,000 net in 2023 — high by historical Japanese standards but still very low by international comparison). Japan's population — which peaked at 128 million in 2010 — is projected to fall to ~88 million by 2065. This represents an almost unprecedented demographic contraction for a peaceful, prosperous nation.

Key evaluative point for exam answers — youth vs elderly dependency: Both high youth dependency and high elderly dependency strain public finances and reduce economic productivity, but in fundamentally different ways. Youth dependency (Niger) requires investment in education, healthcare, and nutrition — spending that can generate future economic returns if the children become healthy, educated, productive adults. Elderly dependency (Japan, UK) requires spending on pensions and healthcare that generates no future economic return. Youth dependency also implies future population growth and the potential for a demographic dividend; elderly dependency implies future demographic decline and permanent fiscal pressure unless offset by immigration. The worst scenario for a country is high total dependency of both types simultaneously — this can occur in post-conflict societies or countries with both very high fertility and improving life expectancy. Bangladesh's current low total dependency is therefore a rare and time-limited demographic opportunity that must be capitalised on through investment in employment, education, and infrastructure.

4.2 Demographic Transition

Changes in Birth Rate and Death Rate Over Time

The central observation underlying all demographic study is that birth rates and death rates do not stay constant — they change systematically as societies develop economically and socially. Understanding the sequence, causes, and pace of these changes is the foundation of the Demographic Transition Model.

The sequence of change: In all documented cases of demographic transition, the death rate falls first. This is because improvements in public health, sanitation, food supply, and basic medicine reduce mortality before the social and economic changes that reduce fertility have taken effect. The gap between a falling DR and a still-high BR produces a period of rapid natural increase — the 'population explosion' — that closes only when BR eventually falls to match DR at a new, lower equilibrium.

Why death rates fall first:

  • Public health interventions: Clean water supply, sewage disposal, and vaccination campaigns can be imposed top-down by governments or delivered by international agencies — they do not require changes in individual behaviour or values. The introduction of clean piped water in 19th-century British cities (following Snow's identification of cholera's waterborne transmission in 1854) dramatically reduced mortality with no change in birth rates. GAVI's vaccination programmes in sub-Saharan Africa have reduced child mortality in Niger, Bangladesh, and across the LIC world since the 1970s — DR falls without any accompanying social development.
  • Medical technology transfer: Antibiotics, vaccines, and oral rehydration therapy can be deployed in LICs very cheaply — a $0.20 sachet of oral rehydration salts prevents deaths from diarrhoea that once killed millions of children. These technologies can be imported from HICs, meaning LICs can achieve mortality reductions that took HICs decades of indigenous development — compressing the DR fall dramatically.
  • Food security improvements: Green Revolution technology (high-yield seed varieties, fertilisers, irrigation) from the 1960s dramatically increased food production in South Asia and parts of Africa, reducing famine mortality. Bangladesh's rice production has tripled since 1970, supporting a much larger population.

Why birth rates fall later — cultural lag: Birth rates are determined by deeply embedded social and cultural values — family size norms, the status of women, religious beliefs, marriage age, and economic calculations about the value of children. These change slowly, over generations, not in response to government programmes alone. There is a structural lag between the fall in DR (rapid, externally driven) and the fall in BR (slow, culturally driven). This lag is the fundamental cause of rapid population growth in LICs — and is why population growth in sub-Saharan Africa continues despite significant development investment.

Changes in BR and DR over time — four countries compared:
Country BR 1960 BR 2023 DR 1960 DR 2023 Life exp. 1960 Life exp. 2023 IMR 1960 IMR 2023
Niger~56~46~30~8~37 yrs~62 yrs~320~68
Bangladesh~47~18~25~6~46 yrs~74 yrs~145~24
UK~17~10~12~9~71 yrs~81 yrs~22~4
Japan~17~7~8~12~68 yrs~84 yrs~30~2

Key patterns: Niger's DR fell dramatically (30→8) while BR fell only slightly (56→46) — the classic Stage 2 gap widening. Bangladesh compressed a full demographic transition into ~40 years. Japan's DR now exceeds BR — Stage 5 natural decrease. The UK's BR and DR have converged at low levels — Stage 4 near-equilibrium.

The Demographic Transition Model — Stages 1 to 5

The Demographic Transition Model (DTM) was originally formulated by Warren Thompson (1929) and elaborated by Frank Notestein (1945), based on the demographic history of Western industrialising nations. It describes a sequence of five stages through which countries pass as they develop economically, characterised by changing relationships between birth rate, death rate, and population size.

Stage 1 — High Stationary

Pattern: Both BR and DR high (~35–40/1,000) and fluctuating. Natural increase near zero. Total population low and relatively stable, but subject to sudden dramatic swings from epidemics, famines, and wars. Population grows very slowly over the long run.

Context: Pre-industrial, subsistence agricultural societies. Life is precarious — death from disease, famine, and conflict is frequent and unpredictable. High BR compensates for high DR: families need many children because most will die before adulthood. No effective contraception; cultural, religious, and economic incentives all favour large families. DR is high but volatile — the Black Death reduced Europe's population by ~30–50% in a decade (1347–53); the 1918 influenza pandemic killed 50–100 million worldwide in months.

Current examples: No modern nation-state remains firmly in Stage 1. Some extremely isolated indigenous communities may approximate this pattern. Pre-colonial societies globally were broadly in Stage 1. Medieval England, pre-industrial France, and pre-contact indigenous Americas all fit this description.

Stage 2 — Early Expanding

Pattern: DR falls sharply and rapidly. BR remains high. The gap between them widens — producing rapid natural increase and fast population growth. Population grows rapidly and the age structure becomes very youthful.

Why DR falls: Improvements in food supply (better agricultural techniques, reduced famines), public health (clean water, sewage disposal, vaccination), and basic medical care reduce epidemic and childhood mortality before any social change reduces fertility. These changes can be externally driven — by colonial administrations, international aid organisations, or technology transfer — without requiring organic socioeconomic development.

Why BR stays high: Cultural lag — social norms, family values, religious beliefs, and economic structures that favour large families do not change quickly. Parents continue having many children because they expect many to die (even as fewer do). The economic rationale for children (farm labour, old-age support) remains intact in agricultural economies.

Current examples: Sub-Saharan Africa broadly, including Niger. Also the pattern seen in most LICs in the 1950s–70s as colonial-era public health improvements reduced DR without accompanying development.

Niger (Stage 2): DR fell from ~30/1,000 (1960) to ~8/1,000 (2023) — driven overwhelmingly by GAVI-funded vaccination (measles, polio, meningitis A), oral rehydration therapy reducing diarrhoeal child mortality, and some improvement in food security through international food aid. IMR fell from ~320 (1960) to ~68 (2023) — a dramatic reduction. Yet BR has barely moved: from ~56 (1960) to ~46 (2023). TFR remains ~6.8 — the world's highest. NIR is ~3.8% — population doubling every 18 years. Niger exemplifies Stage 2 dynamics: mortality reduced by external intervention, fertility unchanged by structural factors. Population has grown from ~3 million (1960) to ~27 million (2023) and is projected to reach ~60–70 million by 2050 — a country already struggling with food security facing more than doubling of its population within 25 years.

Stage 3 — Late Expanding

Pattern: BR begins to fall, while DR continues at a low and stable level. The gap between BR and DR narrows — population continues to grow but at a slowing rate. The age structure begins to mature — a smaller proportion of children, growing working-age cohort.

Why BR falls: The combination of urbanisation (children become costly rather than economically productive), rising female education (delayed marriage, greater career opportunities, increased contraceptive use), improved living standards (less economic need for large families), greater access to and acceptance of contraception, and falling IMR (the child survival hypothesis — parents need fewer children once they are confident those they have will survive). These changes are driven by socioeconomic development and therefore happen more slowly and variably than DR changes.

Current examples: India, much of Latin America, parts of South-East Asia.

Bangladesh (Stage 3 — completing transition): Bangladesh's demographic transition is one of the most remarkable in history — a compression of decades of change into roughly 40 years. TFR fell from ~6.0 (1980) to ~2.0 (2023) — from Stage 2 levels to near-replacement in a single generation, achieved in a country with per-capita income of only ~$2,800. The drivers are instructive: (1) BRAC's integrated development model — the world's largest NGO delivered microfinance, female education, and family planning to rural women simultaneously, changing both economic capacity and social norms; (2) government-funded community health workers — female health assistants (shastho shebika) visited households delivering contraception and maternal health advice directly, removing access barriers; (3) garment industry employment — 4 million workers (~80% women), transforming female economic status, raising the opportunity cost of early marriage and large families; (4) female secondary school stipend programme — government cash transfers conditional on girls staying in school delayed marriage and childbearing; (5) falling IMR — from ~145 (1980) to ~24 (2023), activating the child survival mechanism. BR has fallen from ~47 (1960) to ~18 (2023); DR from ~25 to ~6. Bangladesh is now at or approaching replacement-level fertility — potentially completing its demographic transition within the next decade.

Stage 4 — Low Stationary

Pattern: Both BR and DR are low and relatively stable. Natural increase is near zero or very low. Population is largely stable in size, though often still growing slowly from population momentum (large middle-age cohorts still producing some births). Age structure is mature and increasingly elderly.

Context: Characterises most HICs from roughly the 1970s onwards. Both mortality and fertility have completed their transitions. Life expectancy is high (75–85 years). TFR is below or near replacement (1.4–2.1). Access to healthcare, contraception, and education is universal or near-universal. Economic development has removed the rationale for large families. Population growth, where it occurs, is primarily from immigration.

UK (Stage 4): The UK completed its initial demographic transition by the mid-20th century. By 1960, BR (~17/1,000) and DR (~12/1,000) had both fallen to low levels following industrialisation, public health improvements, and two world wars that altered family formation. A notable post-WWII baby boom (TFR peaked at ~2.9 in 1964) was followed by a sharp fertility decline as women entered the workforce, the contraceptive pill became widely available (from 1961), the Abortion Act (1967) legalised termination, and social norms shifted. TFR fell below replacement (~2.1) in the mid-1970s and has remained there since, reaching ~1.6 in 2023. BR is ~10/1,000, DR ~9/1,000 — near-convergence. Natural increase is minimal (~0.1%); UK population growth is almost entirely driven by net migration (~700,000 net in 2022–23 — historically high). Life expectancy has risen from ~71 years (1960) to ~81 years (2023). The ageing of the baby boom cohort (now 60–78) is driving a rising elderly proportion — from 12% over-65 in 1980 to 19% in 2023, projected to reach 26% by 2045 — beginning to push the DR upward. The UK is approaching the boundary between Stage 4 and Stage 5.

Stage 5 — Decline

Pattern: BR falls below DR — natural decrease. Population begins to shrink without compensating immigration. Age structure is 'constrictive' — narrow base of children, large elderly cohort, shrinking working-age proportion. Population ageing is extreme.

Why DR exceeds BR: DR has risen (not because people are dying younger — life expectancy continues to improve — but because the population is so heavily skewed towards elderly age groups that more deaths occur simply because more elderly people exist). BR remains very low because TFR is far below replacement and the proportion of women of reproductive age is shrinking. This is a structural demographic trap — the very low fertility of previous generations has produced a small young cohort who in turn produce fewer babies.

Current examples: Japan, South Korea, Germany, Italy, Spain, most of Eastern Europe, and a growing list of East Asian nations.

Japan (Stage 5): Japan has been in natural decrease since 2007 — BR (~7/1,000) is now significantly below DR (~12/1,000). This paradox — DR above BR in a country with world-leading life expectancy (~84 years) — illustrates that Stage 5's rising DR is structural, not a health deterioration. Japan's TFR has been below replacement since 1975, reaching ~1.2 in 2023. Population peaked at ~128 million (2010) and had fallen to ~124 million by 2023; projections suggest ~88 million by 2065. The age structure is extreme: 29% over 65 (world's highest proportion), only 11% under 15. The working-age population peaked in ~1995 and is declining rapidly. IMR is ~2 per 1,000 — among the world's lowest — reflecting the near-completion of the epidemiological transition. Japan's trajectory offers a clear picture of what Stage 5 looks like in practice: a shrinking, rapidly ageing population that strains pension and healthcare systems, suffers acute labour shortages, sees declining domestic demand, and faces the existential question of whether its economy and society can function sustainably at a far smaller population size.

A Critical Appreciation of the Demographic Transition Model

The DTM is the most widely used framework in population geography, but it has significant limitations that must be understood for top-mark exam answers. A 'critical appreciation' means recognising both what the model illuminates and where it misleads or oversimplifies.

Strengths of the DTM:

(1) Descriptive power: The model accurately describes the broad demographic history of virtually every country that has industrialised — Western Europe, North America, Japan, and East Asia all followed a recognisable BR/DR transition sequence.

(2) Predictive utility: Countries still in early stages of transition can be expected, broadly, to follow later stages as development proceeds — giving planners a framework for anticipating demographic pressures.

(3) Policy relevance: Identifies the critical 'window' between DR falling and BR falling as the period of maximum population growth — helping target family planning and development investment.

(4) Comparative framework: Allows meaningful comparison of countries at different stages of development by mapping their demographic profiles onto a shared schema.

Limitations and criticisms of the DTM:

(1) Based on Western European experience: The model was derived from the demographic history of industrialising Western nations (England, France, Sweden). It may not accurately describe the pathway of non-Western countries, where the context of transition differs fundamentally — decolonisation, international aid, technology transfer, and globalisation have compressed and altered the sequence. Niger's Stage 2 was triggered by external vaccination programmes, not organic development. Bangladesh's Stage 3 was accelerated by NGO intervention in a way the original model does not capture.

(2) Ignores migration: The DTM considers only natural increase (BR minus DR) and ignores migration as a component of population change. In Stage 4/5 countries, migration is often the dominant demographic force: the UK's population continues to grow despite near-zero natural increase because of net immigration (~700,000/year). Japan's population is declining despite growing (by its standards) immigration because natural decrease overwhelms it. The model is silent on this critical dimension.

(3) Linear and deterministic: The model implies all countries will inevitably pass through all stages in sequence — an assumption embedded in modernisation theory (development is a universal linear pathway). In reality, some countries may follow different trajectories: some skipped rapid industrialisation but still achieved fertility decline through urbanisation and female education (Bangladesh); some may remain in Stage 2 for structural reasons that development theory does not explain (Niger has not begun fertility transition despite 60 years of development investment).

(4) Disease disruption: The HIV/AIDS epidemic reversed DR trends in sub-Saharan Africa from the 1980s–2000s — life expectancy in Botswana fell from ~65 to ~49 years between 1990 and 2003 before antiretroviral therapy reversed this. This disrupted the expected downward DR trajectory, producing patterns the DTM cannot accommodate. Pandemic disease (COVID-19 excess mortality; potential future pandemics) may similarly disrupt the assumed DR decline.

(5) Stage 5 was not in the original model: Thompson and Notestein did not anticipate sub-replacement fertility becoming permanent. Stage 5 was added later to describe Japan, Germany, and Eastern Europe. This suggests the model is being extended post-hoc to fit observed reality rather than predicting it — a weakness in its theoretical foundations. Furthermore, countries are reaching Stage 5 by very different routes: Japan through cultural conservatism and high opportunity cost of childbearing; Germany through reunification disruption and immigration; South Korea through extreme educational competition and housing costs.

(6) Does not explain pace: The model describes the sequence of change but does not explain why some countries transition rapidly (Bangladesh: ~40 years from Stage 2 to Stage 4) while others stall (Niger: 60+ years of development investment with minimal BR change). The mechanisms driving transition speed — female education, governance quality, cultural factors, economic structure — are outside the model itself.

(7) Treats 'development' as automatic and universal: The model assumes development drives demographic change, but does not define or problematise development itself. Countries can receive external mortality reduction (Stage 2) without the socioeconomic development that drives BR decline (Stage 3) — creating a structural trap where population grows rapidly without the economic growth to support it. Niger exemplifies this; the DTM provides no mechanism for explaining why the transition stalls here.

The DTM and our four countries — does the model fit?

Niger: Partially fits Stage 2 — DR has fallen, BR has barely moved. But the model implies Stage 3 will follow as development proceeds. Niger's extreme structural barriers (very low female education, early marriage, political instability, subsistence agriculture, no pension system) suggest Stage 3 may be very distant. The model's implicit optimism about universal progression is challenged by Niger's 60 years of stalled transition.

Bangladesh: Fits the DTM sequence broadly but compressed far faster than the Western model suggests is normal — from Stage 2 to near-Stage 4 in ~40 years. This compression was driven by targeted policy (BRAC, community health workers, female stipends) not organic industrialisation — suggesting the model underestimates the role of deliberate intervention and overstates the need for economic development before BR falls.

UK: Fits Stage 4 well, though the post-WWII baby boom (TFR ~2.9 in 1964) was not predicted by the model — showing that BR can temporarily rise within a 'completed' Stage 4 country. The role of immigration in sustaining population growth is outside the model entirely.

Japan: Fits Stage 5, but the speed and depth of fertility collapse (TFR ~1.2) and the absence of compensating immigration are not explained by the model — which implies Stage 5 is temporary, with population eventually stabilising at a lower level. Japan's trajectory suggests a more permanent demographic contraction than the model anticipates.

Issues of Youthful and Ageing Populations

Issues of youthful populations — Niger (Stage 2)

The demographic profile: Niger has ~50% of its population under 15, a dependency ratio of ~112, and a population growing at ~3.8%/year — doubling every 18 years. From ~27 million today, Niger's population is projected to reach 60–70 million by 2050 and potentially 150+ million by 2100 on current trajectories.

Education pressure: With half the population under 15, Niger must build and staff an enormous number of schools just to maintain current (already very low) enrolment rates — let alone improve them. Niger's adult literacy rate is ~35%, reflecting decades of insufficient educational provision relative to the child population. Each year's birth cohort is larger than the last, requiring ever more teachers, classrooms, and books. Government revenue — constrained by low GDP (~$600 GDP per capita) and high aid dependency — cannot meet this demand. The result: a self-reinforcing cycle in which low education sustains high fertility, which sustains the youth bulge.

Healthcare pressure: Young populations have high rates of childhood infectious disease — diarrhoea, malaria, pneumonia, measles. Niger's health system is chronically underfunded; the doctor-to-population ratio is ~0.04/1,000. Maternal mortality is among the world's highest (~509 per 100,000 live births) — a direct consequence of high fertility and inadequate obstetric care. GAVI vaccination programmes have reduced childhood mortality substantially, but Niger's healthcare system remains overwhelmed by the sheer volume of births.

Food security pressure: Niger is one of the most food-insecure countries in the world — the Global Hunger Index consistently ranks it among the worst. Rapid population growth is increasing pressure on land (average farm sizes are shrinking as land is divided between children), water (the Sahara is advancing), and food supply. The Sahel climate makes agriculture inherently vulnerable to drought; population growth reduces the buffer against crop failure. Niger has experienced repeated food crises (2005, 2010, 2012) in which population growth has outstripped food production — an almost Malthusian scenario.

Employment and the youth bulge: The large cohorts currently aged 0–15 will enter the labour market over the next 10–20 years. Niger's predominantly subsistence agricultural economy cannot absorb millions of new job seekers annually. Without significant economic diversification and job creation, a 'demographic disaster' rather than a dividend is likely — rising unemployment, social instability, and emigration pressure. Youth unemployment is a documented driver of migration toward Libya and across the Mediterranean, and of recruitment by jihadist insurgent groups in the Sahel.

Urbanisation pressure: Rural land pressure is driving urban migration — Niamey (the capital) is growing rapidly. But urban infrastructure (water, sanitation, housing, electricity) cannot keep pace with this influx, producing expanding informal settlements with inadequate services.

The demographic dividend — unrealised potential: If Niger were to achieve a significant fertility decline over the next 20 years, the large cohort currently under 15 could become a working-age majority supporting a smaller dependent population — the demographic dividend. For this to materialise, however, massive investment in girls' education, healthcare, and economic development would need to occur simultaneously with fertility decline. Currently, neither condition is being met.

Bangladesh — transitional position and demographic dividend

Bangladesh is currently experiencing a rare demographic dividend window — a period when the dependency ratio is relatively low (total ~49), the large working-age cohort (20–50 year olds, product of the high fertility 1980s–90s) is economically active, and both youth and elderly dependency are moderate. This dividend has contributed materially to Bangladesh's rapid economic growth (~6–7%/year over the past decade). The garment industry — capitalising on this large pool of working-age women — has driven poverty reduction from ~44% below the poverty line (1991) to ~18% (2022).

However, Bangladesh's demographic dividend is time-limited. As the large current working-age cohort ages, elderly dependency will rise substantially from the 2040s. Bangladesh currently has minimal pension provision — most elderly people depend on family support. Planning for an ageing population while still managing the transition from a youthful one is Bangladesh's key demographic challenge for the coming decades.

Issues of ageing populations — UK (Stage 4) and Japan (Stage 5)

UK — approaching the ageing crisis: The UK has 19% of its population over 65 (2023), rising to a projected 26% by 2045 as the post-WWII baby boom cohort ages. The UK faces the following ageing-related challenges:

  • NHS pressure: Approximately 50% of NHS spending is on over-65s. The baby boom cohort entering their 70s and 80s is driving a surge in demand for hip and knee replacements, cancer treatment, cardiovascular care, and dementia services. The NHS had record waiting lists of ~7.5 million in 2024 — partly a legacy of COVID-19 backlogs, but fundamentally driven by demographic demand outstripping capacity.
  • Pension costs: The state pension costs ~£110bn/year and is triple-locked (rises with the highest of inflation, earnings growth, or 2.5%). As more people claim pensions for longer (a woman retiring at 66 today can expect ~20+ more years of life), costs rise. The government has raised the pension age (to 66, with 67 planned for 2028 and 68 debated) to partially offset this.
  • Social care crisis: Care for elderly people with dementia, mobility limitations, and complex health needs is funded by a combination of local authority budgets and personal assets. Local authority social care budgets have been cut sharply since 2010; means-tested provision means many people must sell their homes to fund care. The UK has a well-documented 'social care crisis' — insufficient care workers (low-paid, high turnover), long waits for care assessments, and a growing population needing support.
  • Labour shortages: A shrinking working-age proportion (partially offset by higher female participation and later retirement) creates labour market pressures. The UK has addressed this partly through immigration — net migration of ~700,000 in 2022–23 predominantly in working-age groups, sustaining the tax base and public sector workforce (particularly healthcare, where ~30% of NHS doctors and nurses are overseas-born).
  • Intergenerational tensions: Older voters (who tend to vote in higher proportions and more conservatively) exert disproportionate political influence, potentially skewing policy toward protecting pensions and healthcare (which benefit the elderly) over housing, education, and climate investment (which benefit younger generations).

Japan — extreme ageing crisis (Stage 5): Japan's ageing challenge is more acute than the UK's in every dimension. With 29% over 65 and a TFR of ~1.2, Japan is experiencing the fastest population ageing of any large nation:

  • Fiscal pressure: Japan spends ~35% of its national budget on social security (pensions and healthcare) — a proportion that will rise as the population ages further. Japan's national debt is ~250% of GDP — the world's highest — partly reflecting decades of borrowing to fund an ageing society.
  • Labour force decline: Japan's working-age population (15–64) peaked at ~87 million in 1995 and will fall to ~52 million by 2060. This structural labour shortage is acute across all sectors — healthcare (there are already ~300,000 unfilled care worker positions), agriculture (average age of Japanese farmers ~67), construction, and manufacturing. Automation and robotics have been deployed extensively but cannot fully substitute for human labour in care-intensive roles.
  • Shrinking population and economic contraction: Japan's population has fallen from ~128 million (2010) to ~124 million (2023) and is projected to fall to ~88 million by 2065. A shrinking population reduces domestic demand, housing values, and economic activity — creating a deflationary spiral that Japan has struggled with since the 1990s. Rural depopulation has accelerated — thousands of villages are being abandoned, schools closed, and infrastructure left unmaintained as elderly residents die and young people move to cities.
  • Policy responses and their limits: Japan has tried pro-natalist policies (baby bonuses, parental leave) with near-zero impact on TFR. It has slowly and reluctantly begun increasing immigration (net immigration of ~400,000 in 2023 — high by Japan's standards, tiny by global comparison — through a new 'Specified Skilled Worker' visa programme). It has raised female labour force participation dramatically (from ~55% to ~73% since 2012 under Abenomics). None of these has reversed the demographic trajectory. Japan's case suggests that once a society enters deep Stage 5 with very low TFR and strong cultural resistance to immigration, there may be no effective short-term policy solution to demographic decline.

Links Between Population and Development: IMR and Life Expectancy Over Time

One of the most important insights of demographic study is that population change and development are deeply interconnected — but the relationship is two-way, complex, and varies by context. The spec specifically requires understanding of how IMR and life expectancy change over time and how these link to the DTM.

Infant mortality rate (IMR) as a development indicator: IMR is the single most sensitive indicator of development because it reflects the intersection of maternal health, healthcare access, nutrition, sanitation, education, and household income all at once. Reductions in IMR drive Stage 2 population expansion (as more children survive, population grows); but IMR reduction also, over time, reduces fertility through the child survival hypothesis (parents need fewer children when they expect those they have to survive). This paradox — that saving children's lives eventually reduces birth rates — is one of the most important and counterintuitive findings of population geography.

IMR change over time — the four countries:

Niger: IMR fell from ~320/1,000 (1960) to ~68/1,000 (2023) — a reduction of ~79%. This dramatic improvement, achieved through GAVI vaccination programmes (measles, meningitis A, rotavirus, polio — all particularly lethal for infants), oral rehydration therapy, and some improvement in maternal care, has been the primary driver of Stage 2 population expansion. With ~27 children dying per 100 live births in 1960, parents needed very large families as insurance. With ~7 dying per 100 today, the insurance rationale is weakening — but the cultural, economic, and structural factors that sustain high fertility have not yet responded. IMR remains the world's 7th highest — reflecting the continued gap in sanitation, maternal healthcare, and nutrition.

Bangladesh: IMR fell from ~145/1,000 (1960) to ~24/1,000 (2023) — a reduction of ~83%, one of the most dramatic improvements in the world at this income level. Key interventions: (1) community health worker home visits delivering oral rehydration therapy — diarrhoea had been the leading killer of children; (2) vitamin A supplementation reducing child mortality from all causes by ~23%; (3) expanded vaccination coverage; (4) improved maternal nutrition and antenatal care. The IMR decline closely tracks the fertility decline — as parents became confident their children would survive, the child survival mechanism activated. Bangladesh's IMR reduction is a model for LIC development — demonstrating that targeted, community-based health interventions can achieve dramatic results at very low cost.

UK: IMR fell from ~22/1,000 (1960) to ~4/1,000 (2023). UK IMR had already fallen substantially before 1960 — from ~150/1,000 in 1900 — driven by improved sanitation (Public Health Act 1875), pasteurisation of milk, improved obstetric care, and the establishment of the NHS (1948). The fall from 22 to 4 reflects continued improvement in neonatal intensive care, prenatal diagnosis, and the virtual elimination of infectious disease as a cause of infant death. UK IMR is now primarily determined by congenital abnormalities and prematurity. Variation by socioeconomic group persists — IMR in the most deprived communities is approximately twice that in the most affluent.

Japan: IMR fell from ~30/1,000 (1960) to ~2/1,000 (2023) — among the world's lowest. Japan's IMR reduction reflects universal healthcare coverage (from 1961), high-quality obstetric and neonatal care, very low rates of poverty, excellent maternal nutrition, and near-universal hospital birth. Japan's ultra-low IMR is consistent with its world-leading life expectancy and represents the near-completion of the epidemiological transition. The contrast with Niger (~68 vs ~2) illustrates the enormous mortality differential between Stage 2 and Stage 5 countries.

Life expectancy as a development indicator: Life expectancy at birth (the average number of years a newborn can expect to live, given current age-specific mortality rates) is the composite product of mortality conditions throughout the life course — infant, child, adult, and elderly mortality combined. It is the single most widely used summary measure of population health.

Global life expectancy rose from ~47 years (1950) to ~73 years (2023) — one of the great achievements of human development, driven by vaccines, antibiotics, better nutrition, and improvements in maternal and child care. This rise in life expectancy is the fundamental driver of the DR fall in Stage 2 and 3 of the DTM. Life expectancy in HICs (~80+ years) is approaching a biological plateau; the remaining gains are concentrated in LICs, where continued reductions in child and maternal mortality offer the largest improvements.

Life expectancy change over time — the four countries:

Niger: Life expectancy rose from ~37 years (1960) to ~62 years (2023) — a gain of 25 years in 60 years, driven almost entirely by falling child and infant mortality (the average was so low historically because so many children died before age 5; adult survival rates have not improved as dramatically). Niger still has one of the world's lowest life expectancies, reflecting continued high IMR, high maternal mortality, malaria burden, malnutrition, and inadequate healthcare for adults. Climate change is adding pressure — heat stress, drought-related food insecurity, and flooding from extreme weather events are increasing adult mortality risks.

Bangladesh: Life expectancy rose from ~46 years (1960) to ~74 years (2023) — a gain of 28 years, one of the largest improvements of any country over this period. This extraordinary gain reflects: the end of colonial underdevelopment and independence (1971); rapid improvements in child survival (IMR fell ~83%); dramatic reduction in famine (the 1974 Bangladesh famine killed ~1 million; no comparable famine has occurred since due to improved food production and distribution); effective community health programmes; rapid poverty reduction. Bangladesh has now achieved life expectancy comparable to some upper-middle income countries, despite being a lower-middle income country — a striking example of development pathways that prioritise human development over GDP growth.

UK: Life expectancy rose from ~71 years (1960) to ~81 years (2023) — a gain of 10 years. The UK's improvement reflects continued advances in cardiovascular medicine (statins, bypass surgery, angioplasty), cancer treatment (earlier diagnosis, improved chemotherapy), reduced smoking rates (from ~45% of adults in 1960 to ~12% in 2023), and improved road safety. However, UK life expectancy growth stalled after 2010 — linked to austerity-related reductions in social care, NHS funding pressures, and rising poverty. Some projections suggest COVID-19 and the ongoing burden of long COVID may permanently reduce UK life expectancy by 1–2 years relative to pre-pandemic trends. UK life expectancy inequality by geography and class remains stark.

Japan: Life expectancy rose from ~68 years (1960) to ~84 years (2023) — a gain of 16 years, reaching the world's highest level. Japan's exceptional life expectancy reflects: universal healthcare (introduced 1961); low obesity rates (~4% — driven by diet, portion sizes, and cultural attitudes); high social cohesion and low inequality (Wilkinson and Pickett's research suggests more equal societies have better health outcomes); very high rates of preventive health checking (regular health screenings are standard); and low rates of violent crime. Japanese women's life expectancy (~87 years) is the world's highest. The rise in DR since the 2000s is entirely structural — a function of the ageing population, not any deterioration in individual health.

The population-development link — three key mechanisms:

  • The child survival hypothesis: Falling IMR → parents have confidence children will survive → fertility falls → NIR falls → population growth slows. This is the central population-development link and explains why development agencies prioritise child survival even though saving children's lives initially accelerates population growth.
  • The demographic dividend: As a country passes through Stage 3, the dependency ratio falls — fewer young dependants, large working-age cohort, not yet many elderly. This creates a window of economic opportunity (East Asia's 'economic miracle' was partly a demographic dividend; Bangladesh is currently in this window). The dividend requires investment in education and employment to realise.
  • The development trap: Countries that reduce mortality without achieving the social and economic development that drives fertility decline (Niger) can become trapped in Stage 2 — rapid population growth without the economic growth to support it. This creates a negative feedback: population growth undermines per-capita income growth, which prevents the development that would reduce fertility. Breaking this trap requires targeted interventions in female education, family planning, and economic development simultaneously — as Bangladesh achieved, but Niger has not.
Key evaluative point — development causes demographic change, or vice versa? The DTM implies development causes demographic change (development → DR falls → BR falls → transition complete). But causality also runs the other way: demographic change enables development (lower fertility → smaller families → more investment per child → higher human capital → faster growth; lower dependency ratio → demographic dividend). Bangladesh illustrates that targeted health and education investment can trigger fertility decline before full economic development — suggesting the causal arrow is not as simple as the DTM implies. For top-mark exam answers, avoid presenting the DTM as a simple causal sequence; instead, present the relationship between development and demographic change as circular, mutually reinforcing, and highly variable in pace depending on governance, culture, and policy.

4.3 Population–Resource Relationships

The Concept of Food Security

Food security exists when all people, at all times, have physical and economic access to sufficient, safe, and nutritious food to meet their dietary needs and food preferences for an active and healthy life (FAO, 1996). It is not simply about whether enough food is produced globally — the world currently produces enough calories to feed ~10 billion people — but whether that food reaches everyone who needs it, in a safe and nutritious form, reliably over time.

The FAO defines food security through four pillars:

  • Availability: Sufficient quantities of food are physically present — produced domestically or imported. Determined by agricultural productivity, land use, climate, and trade flows.
  • Access: People have the economic and physical means to obtain food. Even where food is available, poverty, poor infrastructure, conflict, and market failure can prevent access. Sen's entitlement theory (1981) argued that famines occur not from lack of food but from lack of entitlement — the economic right to acquire food — as demonstrated by the Bengal Famine of 1943 (food was exported during a famine that killed ~3 million).
  • Utilisation: Food is nutritious, safe, and properly absorbed by the body. Requires clean water for preparation, sanitation, healthcare, and knowledge of balanced diets. Food can be available and accessible but still fail on utilisation — e.g. diets high in calories but deficient in micronutrients ('hidden hunger').
  • Stability: Access to food is consistent over time, not disrupted by seasonal shocks, climate events, conflict, or market volatility. Stability is the pillar most vulnerable to climate change and geopolitical instability.

Scale of food insecurity: In 2023, approximately 735 million people faced chronic hunger (FAO) — up from 613 million in 2019, driven by COVID-19 economic disruption, the Ukraine war (disrupting global wheat and fertiliser supply), and escalating conflict in sub-Saharan Africa and the Middle East. A further ~2.4 billion people face moderate food insecurity — lacking consistent access to adequate food. Sub-Saharan Africa has the highest prevalence of undernourishment (~20% of the population). South Asia (Bangladesh, India, Pakistan) still accounts for the largest absolute number of hungry people.

Causes of food shortages:

  • Conflict and war: Armed conflict is the single largest driver of acute food insecurity. War destroys agricultural infrastructure (irrigation systems, storage facilities, markets), displaces farmers from their land, blocks food aid routes, causes hyperinflation making food unaffordable, and diverts government resources from food systems to military spending. The FAO estimates that over 60% of the world's food-insecure people live in conflict-affected areas. This is the 'stability' and 'access' pillars failing simultaneously.
  • Climatic hazards and climate change: Droughts reduce crop yields — the 2011 Horn of Africa drought caused famine in Somalia, killing ~260,000 people. Floods destroy standing crops — Bangladesh's 2022 monsoon floods damaged crops affecting ~7 million people. Cyclones flatten plantations and destroy fishing infrastructure. Climate change is intensifying all three: longer droughts, more intense rainfall, more destructive cyclones, and rising temperatures reducing crop yields in already-hot tropical regions (a 1°C rise reduces wheat yields by ~6% and rice yields by ~3–7%). The Sahel — including Niger — is particularly exposed to rainfall variability and advancing desertification.
  • Poverty and economic access failure: Even where food is physically available, poverty prevents access. When global food prices spiked in 2007–08 (driven by biofuel demand, drought in wheat-producing regions, oil price rises raising fertiliser and transport costs), food riots broke out in 30+ countries — food was present but economically inaccessible to the poor. In LICs, poor households spend 60–80% of income on food, leaving no buffer against price shocks.
  • Poor infrastructure: Post-harvest losses reach 40% in sub-Saharan Africa — food produced but lost to spoilage, pests, and inadequate storage and transport infrastructure. A farmer in rural Niger may produce a surplus but be unable to sell it profitably because roads to markets are impassable in the wet season.
  • Land degradation and soil exhaustion: Soil erosion, salinisation, desertification, and nutrient depletion reduce the productive capacity of agricultural land. The UN estimates ~33% of global soils are moderately to highly degraded. In the Sahel, advancing desertification (partly from overgrazing and deforestation, partly from reduced rainfall) is physically reducing the amount of cultivable land.
  • Population growth outpacing production: In some contexts — particularly sub-Saharan Africa where population is growing fastest — food production per capita has not kept pace with population growth, even if absolute production has risen. Niger's population growth (~3.8%/year) consistently tests the limits of the country's food system, particularly in drought years.
  • Political failures and governance: Food aid can be diverted by governments or armed groups. Price controls can discourage production. Subsidies can distort markets. Land tenure insecurity prevents investment. Corruption undermines food distribution systems.

Consequences of food shortages:

  • Malnutrition: Undernutrition (insufficient calories), protein-energy malnutrition (kwashiorkor, marasmus), and micronutrient deficiency (iron deficiency anaemia, vitamin A deficiency causing blindness, iodine deficiency causing goitre and cognitive impairment). Globally, 149 million children under 5 are stunted (too short for their age — indicator of chronic malnutrition); 45 million are wasted (too thin for their height — acute malnutrition). Malnutrition in the first 1,000 days of life (conception to age 2) causes irreversible cognitive impairment, permanently reducing educational outcomes and economic productivity.
  • Mortality: Severe acute malnutrition is directly lethal — particularly for under-5 children and pregnant women. Famine — the most extreme form of food shortage — is defined by IPC Phase 5 criteria (20%+ households facing extreme food deprivation; acute malnutrition rate 30%+; mortality rates 2/10,000/day for adults or 4/10,000/day for children). Sudan entered IPC Phase 5 famine in 2024.
  • Reduced economic productivity: Malnourished individuals have reduced physical and cognitive capacity, lower educational attainment, and higher healthcare costs — creating a poverty trap in which hunger perpetuates underdevelopment.
  • Political instability and conflict: Food insecurity contributes to social unrest — the 2010–11 Arab Spring was partly triggered by food price spikes. Scarcity of food and water resources is a driver of conflict in the Sahel (farmer-herder conflicts in Niger, Mali, and Burkina Faso over diminishing pasture and water).
  • Forced migration: Famine and food insecurity drive internal displacement (people moving to cities seeking food) and international migration. The Sudan crisis of 2023–24 produced 10+ million displaced people — the largest displacement crisis in the world.
Case study — Sudan: war, famine, and food insecurity (2023–24)

Background: Sudan is a large, potentially agriculturally productive country in north-east Africa with a population of ~48 million. The Nile valley and the clay plains of the Gezira (one of the world's largest irrigation schemes) give Sudan significant agricultural potential — historically it was called the 'breadbasket of the Arab world'. Yet Sudan has experienced repeated food crises, driven overwhelmingly by political rather than physical factors.

The 2023–24 conflict and famine: In April 2023, fighting broke out in Khartoum and across Sudan between the Sudanese Armed Forces (SAF) and the Rapid Support Forces (RSF) — a paramilitary force that grew out of the Janjaweed militias responsible for the Darfur genocide. The conflict rapidly engulfed Khartoum (a city of 6 million), Darfur, Kordofan, and the Gezira — Sudan's agricultural heartland. By mid-2024, over 10 million people had been displaced — the largest internal displacement crisis in the world. The Gezira Scheme, which irrigated ~900,000 hectares and produced much of Sudan's sorghum and wheat, was taken over by the RSF and effectively ceased functioning as fighting damaged infrastructure and farmers fled.
