In August 2024, the Integrated Food Security Phase Classification (IPC) declared famine in the Zamzam IDP camp in North Darfur — the first famine declared anywhere in the world since 2017.

How the conflict caused famine — mechanism by mechanism: (1) Production collapse: Farmers were displaced from fields; the Gezira Scheme was destroyed; livestock herds were looted by armed groups — the primary asset of pastoral communities stripped away. (2) Supply chain destruction: Markets and roads were disrupted or controlled by armed groups; traders could not move food from surplus to deficit areas. (3) Aid blockade: Both the SAF and RSF blocked or diverted humanitarian food aid — aid convoys were looted, aid workers killed, and access negotiations collapsed. By mid-2024, the UN World Food Programme (WFP) had been denied access to most conflict-affected areas. (4) Economic collapse: The Sudanese pound collapsed, triggering hyperinflation — food that was physically present in some markets was economically inaccessible to most of the population. (5) Health system destruction: Hospitals in Khartoum and Darfur were attacked or occupied by armed groups; outbreaks of cholera, measles, and malaria compounded malnutrition, dramatically raising mortality.

Scale of food insecurity: 18 million people (approximately one-third of Sudan's population) faced acute food insecurity at IPC Phase 3 or above by mid-2024; ~5 million at Phase 4 (Emergency); ~500,000 at Phase 5 (Famine). The WFP described it as one of the worst humanitarian disasters of the 21st century.

Evaluation — Malthus vs Sen: Sudan's famine is emphatically not a Malthusian food production crisis — Sudan has agricultural capacity sufficient to feed its population under normal conditions. It is a Senian entitlement failure: war destroyed the social, economic, and political mechanisms by which people access food. This illustrates the most important conceptual point about food insecurity: in the modern world, famine is almost never caused by insufficient global food production but by political failures — conflict, governance collapse, and deliberate obstruction — that destroy food entitlements. A Malthusian framing is dangerously misleading because it implies the solution is reducing population rather than addressing the political and institutional causes of hunger.

Technology, Innovation and Food Production — The Green Revolution

The relationship between technology and food production is central to the Malthus-Boserup debate. Technology — in seeds, farming practices, irrigation, and inputs — has repeatedly expanded the carrying capacity of agricultural land beyond what Malthusian pessimism predicted. The Green Revolution is the most important and most studied example of this process in the 20th century.

The Green Revolution (1960s–1980s) — origins and package

Context: By the early 1960s, rapid population growth across Asia, Latin America, and parts of Africa was generating acute fears of mass famine. Paul Ehrlich's 'The Population Bomb' (1968) predicted that hundreds of millions would starve in the 1970s and 1980s regardless of any crash programmes. This was a moment of peak neo-Malthusian alarm. The Green Revolution — funded largely by the Rockefeller and Ford Foundations and later by the CGIAR (Consultative Group on International Agricultural Research) — was a deliberate, coordinated technological intervention to avert this outcome.

The technological package: The Green Revolution comprised a bundle of complementary innovations: (1) High-Yielding Variety (HYV) seeds: Dwarf wheat varieties developed by Norman Borlaug (Nobel Peace Prize 1970) at CIMMYT in Mexico; dwarf rice varieties (IR8 — 'miracle rice') developed at the International Rice Research Institute (IRRI) in the Philippines. Dwarf varieties produce shorter straw — meaning the plant invests more energy in grain and does not fall over ('lodge') under the weight of a heavy grain head when heavily fertilised. (2) Inorganic fertilisers: HYV seeds were engineered to respond to high fertiliser inputs — they required nitrogen (from synthetic fertiliser) to achieve their yield potential. The Haber-Bosch process, which synthesises ammonia from nitrogen and hydrogen, made synthetic nitrogen fertiliser economically viable; global fertiliser use expanded enormously. (3) Irrigation: HYV seeds also required reliable water supply — seasonal rainfall alone was insufficient for their full yield potential. The Green Revolution therefore required expansion of irrigation infrastructure: tubewells, canals, and reservoirs. In India, irrigated area expanded from ~20m ha (1960) to ~65m ha (2020). (4) Pesticides and herbicides: Monoculture crops (growing a single HYV variety across vast areas) are highly vulnerable to pest and disease outbreaks — requiring chemical crop protection. (5) Mechanisation: Tractors, threshers, and pumps replaced animal and human labour in many contexts, enabling cultivation of larger areas.

The Green Revolution — outcomes, successes, and limitations

Successes and scale of impact: The Green Revolution's achievements were genuinely extraordinary. India's wheat production rose from ~12 million tonnes (1965) to ~75 million tonnes (1990) — more than a six-fold increase in 25 years. India went from chronic food aid recipient to wheat self-sufficiency and then to net exporter. Mexico, where Borlaug first developed HYV wheat, became a net wheat exporter by 1963 having been an importer in the 1940s. The Philippines achieved rice self-sufficiency by the mid-1970s with IR8. Pakistan's wheat yields more than doubled. Bangladesh's rice production tripled between 1970 and 2000 — a direct contributor to ending the large-scale famines that had periodically struck (the 1974 famine was Bangladesh's last). Globally, cereal yields per hectare roughly doubled between 1960 and 2000. The neo-Malthusian prediction of mass famine in the 1970s–80s was averted — Nobel Committee's 1970 citation for Borlaug credited his work with saving over a billion lives.

The Green Revolution and Boserup: The Green Revolution is partly a Boserupian story — population pressure created demand for more food, which drove investment in agricultural research and innovation, which expanded carrying capacity. Boserup's central thesis ('necessity is the mother of invention') is validated in the sense that it was fear of mass starvation that motivated the Rockefeller Foundation and CGIAR's investment. However, it is also a story of deliberate, top-down, externally funded technological intervention rather than organic, bottom-up innovation driven by smallholder farmers themselves — which is closer to Boserup's original mechanism.

Limitations and critiques — environment: (1) Water depletion: The Punjab (India and Pakistan) — the heartland of Green Revolution success — is experiencing an acute groundwater crisis. Intensive irrigation from tubewells has depleted the water table by 0.5m–1m per year in some districts; at current rates, the aquifer may be exhausted within decades. This is profoundly Malthusian: the technology that expanded carrying capacity created a resource constraint (groundwater) that now threatens future production. (2) Soil degradation: Continuous cultivation with heavy chemical inputs has reduced soil organic matter, disrupted soil biology (earthworms, mycorrhizal fungi), caused compaction from machinery, and in some irrigated areas caused waterlogging and salinisation — reducing the long-term fertility of the land. (3) Agrobiodiversity loss: HYV monocultures replaced thousands of traditional varieties adapted to local conditions. The genetic diversity of traditional crop varieties (which carries the raw material for future plant breeding) has been dramatically reduced. (4) Pesticide pollution: Heavy pesticide use has contaminated waterways, reduced biodiversity (pollinator decline), and caused human health impacts in farming communities.

Limitations and critiques — social and economic: (1) Bypassed sub-Saharan Africa: The Green Revolution largely failed to reach sub-Saharan Africa — the region with the greatest food insecurity and the fastest-growing population. HYV varieties were developed for irrigated Asian rice and wheat systems, not for African dryland farming systems (sorghum, millet, cassava, cowpeas). African soils are often more depleted and geologically older than Asian alluvial soils, and African farmers had far less access to irrigation infrastructure, credit for fertiliser, and extension services. (2) Increased inequality: Wealthier, better-connected farmers who could afford HYV seeds, fertiliser, irrigation, and machinery captured most of the benefits. Smallholders and landless labourers often saw their position worsen as larger farms displaced them and falling grain prices (which benefited consumers) reduced the income from their small plots. In some regions, the Green Revolution accelerated rural inequality and landlessness. (3) Input dependency: Green Revolution farming is structurally dependent on purchased inputs — fertiliser (derived from fossil fuels), pesticides (petrochemical), and fossil fuel for irrigation pumps and machinery. Smallholders who adopted HYV systems became dependent on global commodity markets for inputs, exposing them to price shocks (the 2008 fertiliser price spike doubled production costs for smallholders in South Asia and Africa within months).

Green Revolution II — next generation technologies: A second wave of agricultural technological innovation is underway: (1) Genetically modified organisms (GMOs): Bt cotton (insect-resistant) and herbicide-tolerant soybeans are widely grown; Golden Rice (biofortified with Vitamin A) has been approved in some countries. Highly contested — opposition from environmental groups (biodiversity concerns, corporate control) and some LIC governments. (2) Gene editing (CRISPR): More precise than GMO, allows targeted modification without introducing foreign genes. Drought-tolerant, disease-resistant, and nutritionally enhanced varieties under development. (3) Precision agriculture: Satellite imagery, soil sensors, drone-based pesticide application, and GPS-guided machinery allow much more precise application of inputs — reducing waste and environmental impact while maintaining or improving yields. Primarily available to large-scale, capital-intensive farming in HICs. (4) Vertical farming and controlled environment agriculture: Indoor farming using LED lighting and hydroponics can achieve very high yields per unit area with minimal water and no pesticides — but requires enormous energy inputs and capital. Not yet economically viable for staple crops. (5) Agroecology: Farming systems that work with ecological processes rather than against them — intercropping, agroforestry, composting, integrated pest management. Often more resilient than monocultures but typically lower-yielding per unit area. Increasingly promoted for smallholder African farmers. The AGROECOLOGICAL approach represents a Boserupian grassroots innovation path; GMOs and precision agriculture represent a top-down, capital-intensive Borlaug-style path. Both are needed.

Constraints on Sustaining Population — War and Climatic Hazards

Even where food production technology is available and carrying capacity is theoretically sufficient, constraints can prevent populations from being sustained. The spec identifies war and climatic hazards as key constraints.

War as a constraint: Armed conflict disrupts every component of food systems simultaneously — production (farmers flee or are killed; crops are burned as a military tactic; livestock looted), distribution (roads blocked or controlled by armed groups; markets collapse), access (economic collapse and hyperinflation; aid blockaded), and governance (states lose capacity to manage food systems). The Sudan case study above illustrates all of these mechanisms in a single, contemporary example. Historically, deliberate use of starvation as a weapon of war has been documented: the Siege of Leningrad (1941–44, ~800,000 deaths from starvation); the Holodomor (Ukraine, 1932–33, deliberate Soviet collectivisation causing ~3.5–7m deaths); Biafra (Nigeria, 1967–70). Food insecurity also drives conflict — creating a dangerous feedback loop: food scarcity → social instability → conflict → food scarcity. The Sahel exemplifies this: declining rainfall and advancing desertification reduce pasture and water, fuelling farmer-herder conflicts in Niger, Mali, and Burkina Faso that in turn disrupt food production further.

Climatic hazards as a constraint: Climate variability is a fundamental constraint on food production in rain-fed agricultural systems, which predominate in LICs. Drought, flood, and extreme temperatures can reduce crop yields by 50–100% in a single season. Key examples: (1) Sahel drought cycles: The Sahel experienced catastrophic droughts in 1968–73 and 1982–85, causing famines in Niger, Mali, Chad, and Ethiopia that killed hundreds of thousands and inspired the Band Aid/Live Aid campaigns. These droughts were partly natural variability and partly driven by land degradation (deforestation, overgrazing reducing soil moisture retention). (2) El Niño/La Niña: The El Niño Southern Oscillation (ENSO) disrupts rainfall patterns across sub-Saharan Africa, South and South-East Asia, and Latin America — creating drought conditions in some regions and floods in others. The 1997–98 El Niño caused crop failures across eastern and southern Africa. (3) Tropical cyclones: Bangladesh has been repeatedly struck by devastating cyclones — Bhola (1970, ~300,000 deaths including flooding of rice paddies), Sidr (2007), Aila (2009) — each damaging crops, destroying fishing infrastructure, and contaminating freshwater sources with saltwater. Improved early warning systems and cyclone shelters have dramatically reduced direct mortality but crop loss remains significant. (4) Climate change amplification: Climate change is making all climatic hazards more intense and less predictable — extending drought seasons, intensifying monsoons, increasing cyclone energy, and raising sea levels (threatening Bangladesh's delta farmland with saltwater intrusion). The IPCC projects that climate change could reduce crop yields in tropical regions by 25% by 2050 without adaptation.

Other constraints: Beyond war and climate, population sustainability is constrained by: land degradation and soil depletion; water scarcity (70% of global freshwater is used for agriculture; aquifer depletion threatens irrigated agriculture in India, Pakistan, China, and the US High Plains); biodiversity loss (pollinator decline threatens ~30% of global food crops that depend on animal pollination); fossil fuel dependency (modern industrial agriculture requires oil and gas for fertiliser, pesticide, and machinery — making food production vulnerable to energy price shocks); and trade barriers and market failures that prevent food from reaching those who need it.

Carrying Capacity — Malthus, Boserup and the Population–Resource Debate

Carrying capacity is the maximum population that a given area or system can support sustainably, given existing resources and technology. It is not a fixed, absolute number — it changes with technology, management, trade, and environmental conditions. Understanding how carrying capacity is determined, and whether it is fixed or expandable, is the core of the Malthus-Boserup debate and the central question of the population-resource relationship.

Thomas Malthus (1766–1834) — 'An Essay on the Principle of Population' (1798)

Core argument: Malthus observed that human populations have a tendency to grow geometrically (doubling: 1→2→4→8→16) — driven by the universal human drive to reproduce — while food production can grow only arithmetically (adding fixed increments: 1→2→3→4→5). Population therefore always tends to outrun food supply. When population exceeds the food supply, 'positive checks' restore balance: famine (starvation reduces population directly), disease (malnutrition weakens resistance), and war (competition for scarce resources). 'Preventive checks' — delayed marriage, celibacy, and 'moral restraint' — can slow population growth before positive checks become necessary, but Malthus was pessimistic about their effectiveness, particularly among the poor.

Malthus on carrying capacity: Malthus effectively treated carrying capacity as fixed or very slowly expanding — constrained by the finite quantity of cultivable land and the limited rate at which agricultural productivity could improve. He wrote before the Industrial Revolution fully transformed agriculture (before synthetic fertilisers, mechanisation, or the Haber-Bosch process). His model implies a permanent ceiling on population, periodically enforced by famine and disease.

Policy implications of Malthusianism: Malthus drew deeply conservative policy conclusions: poor relief (the Elizabethan Poor Laws) was counterproductive because it allowed the poor to reproduce more, ultimately increasing misery. Population control (whether through delayed marriage or, in neo-Malthusian extensions, contraception) is necessary to avoid catastrophe. These implications have been used to justify cruel policies — opposition to famine relief, forced sterilisation, and, in the 20th century, coercive population control programmes in LICs.

Neo-Malthusians: Paul Ehrlich ('The Population Bomb', 1968) predicted hundreds of millions would starve in the 1970s–80s. The Club of Rome's 'Limits to Growth' (1972) used computer modelling to argue that industrial civilisation would collapse within a century from resource exhaustion. Garret Hardin's 'Tragedy of the Commons' (1968) argued that shared resources are inevitably overexploited. These thinkers extended Malthus beyond food to include oil, water, minerals, and the atmosphere's capacity to absorb greenhouse gases. Climate change — the global atmosphere's carrying capacity for CO2 being exceeded — is the most compelling contemporary neo-Malthusian argument.

Ester Boserup (1910–1999) — 'The Conditions of Agricultural Growth' (1965)

Core argument: Boserup inverted the Malthusian relationship: rather than food supply limiting population, population growth drives agricultural innovation. As population density increases, the pressure on land intensifies, and farmers are forced to adopt more labour-intensive and more productive farming systems. Boserup described an agricultural intensification sequence: from long-fallow forest shifting cultivation (land rested for 20+ years) → short-fallow (3–10 years) → annual cropping → multi-cropping (two or more crops per year on the same land). Each stage requires more labour, more capital, and more technological innovation, but produces more food per unit area. The driving force is necessity — 'necessity is the mother of invention.' Carrying capacity therefore expands endogenously as population grows, without external intervention.

Evidence for Boserup: The intensification of farming in East and South-East Asia as population densities rose — from swidden agriculture to wet rice cultivation to triple-cropping — is broadly consistent with Boserup's model. The Green Revolution itself can be seen as Boserupian: population pressure on food supply created the demand for new technology; CGIAR's research investment was funded because mass starvation was feared; farmers adopted HYV seeds because they needed more food from the same land. The 10,000-year history of agriculture — from hunter-gatherer (very low carrying capacity) to modern industrial farming (very high carrying capacity per unit area) — can be read as a continuous Boserupian intensification sequence driven by population growth.

Limitations of Boserup: (1) Boserup's model is based primarily on pre-industrial, smallholder farming systems — it may not describe modern capital-intensive agriculture, where innovation is driven by profit rather than population pressure. (2) The model assumes that innovation will always be sufficient and timely — but there is no guarantee that solutions will arrive before irreversible damage to soils, water, or ecosystems occurs. (3) The model does not address distributional questions — even if aggregate food production expands, it may not reach those who need it (as Sudan and the Bengal famine demonstrate). (4) Some traditional farming populations subject to extreme pressure have not innovated but collapsed — Easter Island is the classic parable (Boserupian innovation failed; population overshoot led to resource exhaustion and social collapse, though the Easter Island narrative is now contested by historians). (5) Innovation requires enabling conditions — secure land tenure, access to credit, education, infrastructure, and political stability — that are absent in many LICs. In Niger, population pressure has not driven agricultural innovation but instead land degradation, migration, and food insecurity, partly because these enabling conditions are absent.

Comparing Malthus and Boserup — a structured evaluation
Dimension Malthus Boserup
Causal directionFood supply limits populationPopulation growth drives food innovation
Carrying capacityFixed or slowly expanding; a ceilingExpandable; rises with population pressure
View of population growthDangerous; leads to miseryStimulating; drives progress
TechnologyCannot keep pace with population foreverContinuously expanded by necessity
TonePessimisticOptimistic
Policy implicationControl population growthEnable and trust human innovation
Green Revolution as evidenceHYV success temporary; groundwater depletion now Malthusian threatInnovation averted famine; validates thesis
Sudan as evidencePopulation pressure on limited resources contributedCrisis is political not technological — Boserup not applicable here
Niger as evidencePopulation growth outpacing food production — positive checks activeInnovation absent because enabling conditions (land rights, credit, stability) are missing

Evaluative conclusion: Both theories capture partial truths. Boserup better describes the long-run pattern of agricultural history — technology has continuously expanded carrying capacity beyond what Malthus thought possible. Malthus better describes short-run crises and the reality that technology requires enabling conditions that are not universally present. The Green Revolution validates Boserup globally but simultaneously creates Malthusian groundwater and soil constraints locally. Sudan validates neither — it is primarily a political and institutional failure, not a population-resource relationship at all. The most defensible position is that carrying capacity is expandable but not infinitely so; innovation can outrun population growth but only with the right governance, investment, and institutional conditions; and that distribution and entitlement — not absolute production — determine whether people go hungry.

Optimum Population, Overpopulation and Underpopulation — A Critical Evaluation

Optimum population is the theoretical population size at which the well-being or per-capita output of a population is maximised, given existing resources, technology, and economic organisation. Below the optimum, resources are underexploited (underpopulation); above it, per-capita output falls and living standards decline (overpopulation).

Overpopulation occurs when a population exceeds the carrying capacity of its resource base at a given level of technology — meaning the environment and economy cannot sustainably support the population at an acceptable standard of living. Indicators include: falling per-capita food production; rising unemployment; environmental degradation (deforestation, soil erosion, water depletion); emigration driven by resource pressure; declining living standards.

Underpopulation occurs when a region has more resources than its current population can effectively exploit — too few people to develop resources, fill economic roles, defend territory, or maintain infrastructure. Indicators include: labour shortages; large tracts of unused productive land; declining public service viability (schools and hospitals closing as population falls); economic stagnation. Classic examples: 19th-century Canada and Australia (settlers actively recruited); rural Japan today (villages with more houses than people); Siberia (vast resources, very sparse population).

Critical evaluation of the optimum population concept:

  • The optimum is not fixed: Because both resources and technology change, the optimum population changes continuously. The UK in 1700 (pre-industrial, population ~5 million) was perhaps at or near its agricultural optimum; by 1900 (industrialised, population ~37 million), technology and trade had expanded the optimum dramatically. Today (population ~68 million, largely fed by imported food), the UK's domestic 'optimum' is difficult to define without reference to global trade. A country's optimum cannot be determined independently of its position in global markets.
  • Optimum for whom and by what measure? 'Per-capita output' is a narrow measure of wellbeing. A smaller population might maximise per-capita GDP but at the cost of lower cultural diversity, reduced innovation (which correlates with urban density and population size), and less geopolitical influence. Japan's declining population may raise per-capita income in the short run (as the capital stock is shared among fewer people) but threatens long-run innovation and economic dynamism. The 'optimum' depends entirely on what you are optimising for.
  • Overpopulation is often a governance failure, not a population problem: Many supposedly 'overpopulated' countries have vast unexploited resources. Nigeria has more cultivable land per capita than the Netherlands (which feeds ~170 million people through exports on a fraction of Nigeria's land area). The difference is not population density but governance quality, infrastructure, and institutional capacity. Calling Nigeria 'overpopulated' obscures the political causes of food insecurity. Similarly, Sudan is not 'overpopulated' — it is misgoverned and conflict-riven.
  • Underpopulation carries its own costs: Very low population density makes infrastructure provision (roads, schools, hospitals, broadband) extremely expensive per capita. Rural Japan and eastern Germany are experiencing school closures, hospital closures, and infrastructure decay as populations fall below the viability threshold for public services. Declining populations also lose the agglomeration benefits of urban density — innovation, knowledge spillovers, and specialisation that drive productivity growth.
  • The concept ignores distribution: A country can be simultaneously 'underpopulated' (too few people to exploit all resources) and have millions going hungry (because access to food is distributed unequally). Brazil is theoretically underpopulated relative to its agricultural potential, yet has ~20 million food-insecure people. The optimum population concept treats population as an aggregate, ignoring the distributional question of who benefits from resources.
  • The ecological dimension: Traditional optimum population theory focuses on economic output per capita. A more contemporary framing asks: what population can the Earth support within planetary boundaries — the ecological limits of climate stability, biodiversity, water cycles, and soil health? The Global Footprint Network calculates humanity's ecological footprint currently exceeds Earth's biocapacity by ~75% — suggesting we are collectively in overshoot in ecological terms, regardless of whether per-capita economic output is maximised. This is a neo-Malthusian framing applied at global scale.
Optimum, overpopulation, underpopulation — applied to case studies:

Niger: By most conventional measures, Niger appears overpopulated relative to its current agricultural carrying capacity — population growth is outstripping food production, soils are degrading, and emigration is rising. However, a Boserupian would argue that Niger is underdeveloped relative to its potential carrying capacity: with appropriate investment in drip irrigation (the Sahel receives some solar energy-driven groundwater recharge), improved seeds adapted to arid conditions (AGRA's work on drought-tolerant varieties), and governance reform, Niger's carrying capacity could be substantially expanded. Whether it is overpopulated or simply under-developed is a question with profound policy implications — the former suggests population control; the latter suggests development investment.

Sudan: Sudan is not overpopulated by any objective measure — it has large areas of potentially productive agricultural land (the Nile valley, the Gezira, the Sudd). Its food crisis is political. Applying the 'overpopulation' label to Sudan would be analytically misleading and politically dangerous — it would imply the solution is population reduction rather than conflict resolution and governance reform.

Japan: Japan is increasingly underpopulated relative to its economic needs — a shrinking workforce is creating labour shortages across all sectors. Yet its population density is very high (336 people/km²) — illustrating that 'underpopulation' is not about density but about the ratio of population to economic demand and resource capacity. Japan needs more working-age people, not necessarily more land or resources.

UK: The UK's optimum is difficult to determine. With ~68 million people, it is densely populated but highly productive and technologically advanced. It imports ~48% of its food, making its effective carrying capacity dependent on global trade — meaning its domestic 'optimum' has no clear meaning in isolation. Whether Brexit-related trade disruptions, climate impacts on global food systems, or energy price shocks will test UK food security in coming decades is a genuinely open question.

Key evaluative conclusion for exam answers: The concepts of carrying capacity, optimum population, overpopulation, and underpopulation are analytically useful starting points but must be handled with care. All three depend on the level of technology (which changes), the governance quality (which determines whether resources are effectively used), the distribution of access to resources (which determines whether benefits reach everyone), and the scale of analysis (local vs national vs global). Malthus and Boserup each capture important partial truths — Malthus that there are real resource limits, Boserup that those limits are continuously expanded by human ingenuity. The most important insight — supported by Sudan, the Green Revolution's both successes and side-effects, and Niger's stalled transition — is that hunger and food insecurity in the modern world are primarily political and institutional failures, not simple population-resource arithmetic. Technology can feed the world; the challenge is creating the governance, distribution, and institutional conditions to ensure it does.

4.4 The Management of Natural Increase

China's One-Child Policy — Case Study

Background and context: By 1979, China's population had reached 975 million, growing at ~2% per year. Chairman Mao had encouraged large families ('more people, more power') through the 1950s–60s, leading to the baby boom generation. Deng Xiaoping's reformist government, focused on economic modernisation, concluded rapid population growth threatened development goals. The One-Child Policy (OCP) — formally the 'Family Planning Policy' — was introduced nationally in 1980.

Implementation and Mechanisms

  • Economic incentives: Families with one child received monthly cash bonuses, priority access to housing, healthcare, and education. Families with 2+ children faced fines of 2–6× annual income (the 'social maintenance fee').
  • Workplace penalties: Government employees could lose jobs or promotions for having a second child.
  • Community enforcement: Local officials given population targets; 'barefoot doctor' community health workers provided contraception and monitored pregnancies. Social pressure and public shaming used.
  • Compulsory measures: In many rural areas, forced sterilisation and forced abortions were reported — particularly in the 1980s and 1990s. China's government officially denied these as policy but acknowledged abuses by local officials under pressure to meet targets.
  • Exemptions: Rural families whose first child was a girl could have a second child (reflecting the economic value of sons in agriculture). Ethnic minorities (Uyghurs, Tibetans, Mongolians) were initially exempt (though restrictions on minority reproduction became a later controversy). Twins always permitted.

Demographic Outcomes

  • TFR fell from ~2.8 (1979) to ~1.5 (2015) — a substantial decline, though much of this would have occurred anyway with urbanisation and development (China's peers in East Asia — Taiwan, South Korea — had similar fertility declines without coercive policies).
  • NIR fell from ~1.5% (1980) to ~0.5% (2015), slowing population growth significantly.
  • Population reached 1.4 billion rather than an estimated 1.7 billion without the policy.
  • China's 'demographic dividend' — a large working-age population supporting a smaller dependent population — contributed to economic growth ('Chinese economic miracle') from 1980–2010.

Problems and Unintended Consequences

Gender imbalance: Sex ratio at birth reached 117 boys per 100 girls (natural ratio is ~105:100). Driven by cultural preference for male heirs (sons carry family name, provide old-age support in Confucian culture), combined with ultrasound technology enabling sex-selective abortions and female infanticide. By 2020, China had ~30–35 million 'missing women' — men who cannot find wives. This has social consequences: higher crime rates, trafficking, psychological pressure on men.
The '4-2-1 problem': Under the OCP, an only child ('little emperor' generation) grows up to potentially support 2 parents and 4 grandparents financially. With weakening family-based elder care and an underdeveloped pension system, this creates enormous individual and state financial pressure.
Ageing population crisis: China's working-age population peaked in 2011. By 2050, ~26% of China's population will be over 65. The pension system faces an actuarial crisis. Labour shortages are already emerging in manufacturing, raising costs and undermining the competitive advantage that drove China's growth. The OCP created exactly the demographic structure it was designed to avoid — a future where population growth threatens stability — just delayed by 40 years.
Human rights violations: The OCP was enforced through methods widely condemned by international human rights organisations — forced sterilisations, forced abortions (some in the 9th month), destruction of property for non-payment of fines. China's government acknowledged 'excesses' at local level. The policy generated significant international criticism and diplomatic friction.

Policy Relaxation and Reversal

Two-child policy (2015): In recognition of ageing crisis and demographic imbalance, the OCP was relaxed to allow all couples two children. However, by 2015, urban Chinese couples had internalised small-family norms — the cost of living, competitive education culture ('involution'), housing costs, and career pressures all discouraged second children. Birth rates did not significantly recover.

Three-child policy (2021): Extended to three children following 2020 Census showing sharper-than-expected population decline. Accompanied by policies reducing education costs, extending parental leave, and subsidising childcare. TFR remained ~1.09 (2022) — one of the world's lowest.

Population crisis now: China's population is now declining — fell by 850,000 in 2022, the first decline since the great famine of 1961. The government faces the paradox of having spent 35 years suppressing births and now being unable to stimulate them. This mirrors the experience of Singapore, South Korea, and Japan — once lost, fertility is extraordinarily difficult to recover in an urbanised, high-cost society.

Evaluation: China's OCP illustrates several key themes: (1) Population policies have long time-lags — effects of the OCP are still unfolding decades later. (2) Unintended consequences (gender imbalance, ageing) can be as damaging as the problem the policy addressed. (3) Coercive policies raise fundamental human rights questions about bodily autonomy and state power. (4) Even successful fertility reduction (in terms of lowering the NIR) can create new demographic problems. (5) Cultural and economic factors ultimately determine fertility more than state policy — hence the failure to raise TFR after 2015. (6) The policy cannot be fully separated from China's development: some of China's economic success came from the demographic dividend it created, but that dividend is now exhausted. A genuinely complex, multidimensional case study with no simple verdict.

5.1 Migration as a Component of Population Change

Movements of Populations — Types and Definitions

Migration is the movement of people from one place to another with the intention of settling, either permanently or for at least one year. The CIE specification explicitly excludes movements of less than one year's duration — short-term mobility such as daily commuting, seasonal agricultural work, or tourism. This one-year threshold distinguishes migration from circulation (temporary, repetitive movement without a permanent change of residence, such as nomadism or seasonal transhumance).

Classification by origin and destination:

  • Internal migration — movement within a country's borders. The dominant form of migration globally by volume. Examples: rural-to-urban migration in India and China; counter-urbanisation in the UK; movement of people from the US 'rust belt' to southern 'sun belt' states.
  • International migration — movement across a national border. Subdivided into: emigration (leaving a country) and immigration (arriving in a country). The difference between the two — net migration — adds to or subtracts from a country's population. Net migration has exceeded natural increase as the primary driver of UK population growth since the late 1990s.
  • Inter-regional migration — movement between regions within a country, distinct from local intra-regional movement.

Classification by degree of choice:

  • Voluntary migration — movement primarily driven by the migrant's own decision, seeking better opportunities. The migrant retains meaningful agency. Most economic migration (rural-to-urban in India, Polish workers to the UK post-2004) is classified as voluntary, though economic desperation can blur the line.
  • Forced (involuntary) migration — movement under compulsion where the migrant has no realistic alternative. Includes refugees fleeing persecution, people displaced by conflict, and victims of trafficking. The distinction from voluntary migration is not always sharp — a Sahel farmer whose land is failing due to drought occupies a grey area between economic choice and environmental compulsion.

Key forced migration categories:

  • Refugee — defined by the 1951 UN Refugee Convention as a person outside their country of origin with a well-founded fear of persecution based on race, religion, nationality, political opinion, or membership of a particular social group. Refugees are protected by international law, including the principle of non-refoulement (cannot be forcibly returned to a country where they face persecution). As of 2024, ~43 million people globally hold refugee status — the highest number ever recorded. The largest source countries are Syria (~6.4m), Afghanistan (~6.1m), and Ukraine (~6m, following Russia's 2022 full-scale invasion).
  • Asylum seeker — a person who has applied for refugee status and is awaiting a legal determination. Not yet legally recognised as a refugee but protected from deportation during the process. The UK had ~175,000 pending asylum applications in 2024, reflecting significant backlogs in the Home Office decision-making process.
  • Internally displaced person (IDP) — forced to flee home but remains within their own country. IDPs are not protected by the 1951 Refugee Convention (which only applies to people who cross an international border) and are often harder to assist because the government of their own country may be the source of persecution. As of 2024, Sudan's civil war created over 10 million IDPs — the world's largest internal displacement crisis.

Classification by duration: Beyond the one-year threshold that defines migration (vs circulation), migrations can be classified as: permanent (no intention to return); long-term (one year or more but with intention to return eventually); and circular migration (repeated moves between origin and destination, common among guest workers — e.g. Bangladeshi construction workers in the Gulf who spend 2–3 years working before returning home, often to repeat the cycle).

Causes of Migration — Lee's Push-Pull Model (1966)

Everett Lee's model, published in 1966, remains the most widely used framework for analysing migration causes. Lee argued that migration decisions are shaped by four interacting sets of factors: push factors at the origin, pull factors at the destination, intervening obstacles between origin and destination, and personal factors that vary between individuals.

The four components of Lee's model:

1. Push factors — conditions at the origin that repel people or reduce the attractiveness of staying. They create a motivation to leave. Examples:

  • Economic: Unemployment or underemployment; low wages; lack of land (land fragmentation in South Asia as farms are subdivided between sons); limited economic diversification (single-crop economies vulnerable to price shocks). Example: Rural Punjab farmers in the 1960s–80s, despite Green Revolution gains, faced land fragmentation and sought industrial employment in cities.
  • Environmental: Drought, desertification, flooding, soil exhaustion, and climate-related crop failure. Example: Recurring Sahel droughts drove large-scale migration from Niger, Mali, and Burkina Faso to cities and towards Europe. The 1984–85 Ethiopian drought displaced millions internally and created 1 million refugees.
  • Social: Discrimination, ethnic or religious persecution, limited access to education or healthcare. Example: The Rohingya Muslim minority fled Myanmar from the 2017 military crackdown — ~700,000 crossed into Bangladesh within weeks.
  • Political: Conflict, war, authoritarianism, government persecution, gang violence. Example: Syria's civil war (from 2011) displaced ~13 million people — half the pre-war population — creating the largest refugee crisis since WWII.

2. Pull factors — conditions at the destination that attract migrants. They create a reason to move to a specific place. Examples:

  • Economic: Higher wages; more and better job opportunities; growing industries needing labour. Example: The UK's post-2004 labour market (low unemployment, rising wages in construction and hospitality) attracted ~1.5–2 million Polish workers following EU accession.
  • Social: Existing migrant community networks; better educational institutions; higher quality healthcare. Example: The presence of established Bangladeshi communities in Tower Hamlets, London, pulled subsequent migrants through chain migration — by 2021, ~32% of Tower Hamlets residents were Bangladeshi-born or of Bangladeshi heritage.
  • Political: Political stability; liberal asylum systems; rule of law. Example: Germany's relatively generous asylum system and large existing Turkish and Syrian diaspora make it the primary EU destination for Syrian asylum seekers (~700,000 Syrian refugees accepted 2015–16).
  • Environmental: More favourable climate; lower natural hazard risk; better land. Example: US internal migration from the cold, industrial 'rust belt' (Michigan, Ohio, Pennsylvania) to the warmer 'sun belt' (Florida, Texas, Arizona) — partly climate-driven alongside economic factors.

3. Intervening obstacles — barriers between origin and destination that impede migration even when push and pull factors are strong. They filter who migrates and how far.

  • Cost: Travel costs, agent fees, visa fees, and the cost of establishing oneself in a new location. For many would-be migrants from sub-Saharan Africa, the cost of reaching Europe (~$2,000–5,000 paid to smugglers across the Sahara and Mediterranean) is prohibitive, filtering migration to those who can raise capital — often not the poorest.
  • Distance: The further the destination, the higher the cost and uncertainty. Most migrants move to nearby destinations — internal before international; a regional city before the national capital or overseas (see distance decay, below).
  • Immigration controls and national borders: Visa requirements, work permit quotas, border walls, and deportation regimes are perhaps the most significant contemporary intervening obstacles for international migration. The US-Mexico border — with over 700 miles of fencing, surveillance technology, and ~20,000 Border Patrol agents — acts as a major physical and institutional barrier, though it has not stopped migration but diverted it to more dangerous routes. The UK's post-Brexit points-based immigration system creates different barriers for different nationalities and skill levels.
  • Language and cultural barriers: Migrants to countries with unfamiliar languages face significant integration costs — limiting destination choices to countries sharing a language (Mexicans to USA; Poles to UK/Germany/Ireland; Algerians to France) or where English as a global language provides a bridge.
  • Physical barriers: Mountains (Alps limiting north-south movement in Europe), seas (the English Channel; the Mediterranean), and deserts (the Sahara as a barrier to sub-Saharan African migration to North Africa and Europe) all impose costs and risks that filter migration flows.
  • Information barriers: Migrants require knowledge of conditions at the destination — job availability, living costs, visa requirements, legal rights. Lack of information increases uncertainty and risk, particularly for pioneering migrants. Chain migration (see below) reduces this barrier for subsequent migrants.
  • Family and social ties at origin: Attachment to community, family responsibilities (elderly parents; young children), land ownership, and cultural identity all create inertia — a reluctance to leave even under strong push conditions.

4. Personal factors — individual characteristics that determine how strongly a person responds to push and pull factors and how effectively they can overcome intervening obstacles.

  • Age: Young adults (18–35) are most likely to migrate — they have the most to gain from higher wages over a long career, have fewer sunk costs (established careers, children in school, mortgages), and are more physically capable of long journeys. Elderly people rarely migrate voluntarily, except in retirement moves (UK retirees to Spain; US retirees to Florida).
  • Education and skills: Educated, skilled workers face fewer immigration barriers (points-based systems actively recruit them), have more earning potential at destinations, and have better access to information about opportunities. Unskilled workers face greater obstacles and earn less of a wage premium. This creates a 'brain drain' — LICs lose their most educated people to HIC labour markets (e.g. ~50% of Ghanaian-trained doctors practice overseas).
  • Gender: Historically, international migration was male-dominated. Increasingly, women migrate independently for domestic work, nursing, and manufacturing employment (Philippine and Indonesian domestic workers in the Gulf; Bangladeshi garment workers moving to Dhaka). However, gender norms, safety concerns, and legal restrictions still make migration more difficult and risky for women in many contexts.
  • Wealth: Migration requires capital for travel, agent fees, and establishment costs. Counterintuitively, the very poorest rarely make long-distance international moves — they lack the resources. The 'migration hump' describes how, as incomes rise in LICs from very low levels, migration first increases (people can now afford to move) before eventually decreasing (as domestic opportunities improve). This is why emigration from Mexico and South Korea peaked at middle-income levels.
  • Family stage: Single people and young couples without children are most mobile. Families with school-age children face much higher costs and disruption from moving, creating significant inertia.
Critical evaluation of Lee's push-pull model:

Strengths: (1) Provides a clear, accessible framework for classifying migration determinants — useful as an exam organising structure. (2) Explains why people facing identical conditions make different decisions (personal factors and differential perception of obstacles). (3) Recognises that migration is not simply about absolute conditions but about the perceived difference between origin and destination. (4) Obstacles component is particularly valuable — it shows why migration is not simply a response to inequality (if it were, far more people would migrate from LICs to HICs).

Limitations: (1) Treats migrants as fully rational individual actors — in reality, migration decisions are made under imperfect information, shaped by social networks, cultural norms, and historical migration patterns that the model does not capture. (2) Ignores structural forces — colonialism, trade relationships, and the unequal global economic system shape migration flows in ways that cannot be reduced to individual push-pull calculations. Mexico's migration to the USA is inseparable from NAFTA's destruction of Mexican smallholder agriculture; Bangladesh's migration patterns are inseparable from the global garment industry. (3) Static — the model presents a snapshot of conditions at a moment in time but doesn't explain how migration flows evolve and self-perpetuate over time through network effects. (4) The distinction between push and pull is often artificial — the same factor (e.g. a wage differential) appears simultaneously as a push from the origin and a pull to the destination. (5) Doesn't explain the persistence of migration streams after initial conditions change — Polish migration to the UK continued at high levels even as Polish wages rose substantially post-2004, maintained by established networks. Lee's model must therefore be supplemented by the Todaro model (which addresses decision-making under uncertainty) and migration network theory (which explains self-perpetuating flows).

The Todaro Model of Rural-Urban Migration (1969)

Michael Todaro's model, developed in 1969 to explain rural-to-urban migration in LICs, is a critical refinement of the push-pull approach. It addresses a paradox that puzzled development economists: why does rural-urban migration continue — and often accelerate — even when urban unemployment is high and visibly growing? Under a simple push-pull model, high urban unemployment should act as a deterrent to further migration. Todaro observed the opposite — migrants continued to flood into cities like Nairobi, Lagos, and Dhaka even as slums expanded and formal urban jobs remained scarce.

Todaro's core argument — expected income, not actual income:

Todaro proposed that migration decisions are driven not by the actual urban wage but by the expected income — calculated as: expected urban income = urban wage × probability of finding formal employment.

A rational migrant from rural Kenya earning $1/day compares this not with the formal urban wage ($10/day) but with the probability-weighted expected income from migrating. If there is a 30% chance of securing a formal job within one year, the expected income is $10 × 0.3 = $3/day — still three times the rural wage. Migration therefore remains rational even with 70% urban unemployment, as long as the urban wage premium is large enough. This explains why urban in-migration continues despite high unemployment: the rural-urban wage gap in many LICs is so large (often 5–10×) that even a modest probability of formal employment justifies the move.

Furthermore, Todaro noted that migrants do not arrive in the formal sector immediately — they typically enter the informal sector first (street trading, domestic service, casual construction work, motorcycle taxi driving), earning more than the rural alternative while waiting for a formal opportunity. The informal sector acts as a waiting room and acclimatisation zone for formal employment. This explains the coexistence of rapid urban growth with high measured unemployment — many 'unemployed' urban migrants are actually informally employed at incomes above their rural alternative.

Example: In Lagos, Nigeria, formal employment in multinational companies or government pays ~5–10× the rural farm wage. The informal sector (hawking, artisanal services, waste collection, motorcycle taxis — 'okadas') pays ~2–4× the rural alternative. Even amid Lagos's visible urban poverty and crowding, the expected income from migrating remains positive — which is why Lagos continues to receive ~300,000 net migrants per year despite already having 15–20 million residents, chronic infrastructure failure, and visible unemployment.

Todaro's policy implications — and why standard interventions fail:

A conventional policy response to urban overcrowding is to create formal jobs in cities — expand manufacturing, attract foreign investment. Todaro showed this is likely to be counterproductive: creating 100 formal urban jobs increases the perceived probability of formal employment for all rural migrants, potentially attracting 200+ new migrants for every 100 jobs created. Net urban unemployment may actually rise. The policy implication is counterintuitive: to reduce urban overcrowding, you must improve rural livelihoods — raise agricultural productivity, improve rural infrastructure, and reduce the rural-urban wage gap at its source. If rural incomes rise, the expected gain from migration falls and rural-urban flows slow. This is why agricultural development, rural electrification, and rural road-building are migration management tools as much as they are agricultural policy — a point with direct relevance to India's MGNREGA rural employment guarantee scheme and China's investment in rural infrastructure as tools for managing migration pressure.

Comparing Lee and Todaro — key distinctions for exam answers:
Dimension Lee (1966) Todaro (1969)
ScopeAll types of migrationRural-urban migration in LICs specifically
Key driverBalance of push and pull factorsExpected (probability-weighted) income difference
Treats migrants asResponding to perceived conditionsRational economic calculators under uncertainty
Role of unemploymentUrban unemployment = deterrent (obstacle)Urban unemployment does NOT deter if wage premium large enough
Informal sectorNot explicitly addressedCentral — the waiting room for formal employment
Policy implicationAddress push factors or enhance pull; reduce obstaclesCreating urban jobs increases migration; improve rural livelihoods instead
Explains continuing migration despite urban unemployment?No — a limitationYes — its central contribution

Processes of Migration

Chain migration: Chain migration occurs when pioneer migrants who have established themselves at a destination provide information, accommodation, employment contacts, and social support for subsequent migrants from the same origin community. Each wave of migrants reduces the effective intervening obstacles for the next — the uncertainty of the unknown destination is replaced by the security of a known network. Chain migration creates self-reinforcing migration streams that persist long after the original push-pull balance that initiated them has changed, because the network itself becomes a pull factor.

Chain migration explains why particular ethnic and national groups concentrate in specific locations: Bangladeshis in Tower Hamlets, London (early migrants from Sylhet in the 1960s–70s created a network that drew tens of thousands of subsequent migrants from the same region); Mexicans in East Los Angeles; Somalis in Minneapolis–St Paul (initial refugee resettlement created a community that subsequently attracted further Somali migration from across the USA). Chain migration also explains why many migration flows are spatially very specific at both ends — not simply 'rural Mexico to USA' but particular villages in Oaxaca to particular neighbourhoods in Los Angeles, linked by family and community networks built over decades.

Stepped migration: Stepped migration describes movement in stages up the settlement hierarchy rather than a single long-distance move. A subsistence farmer in rural Bangladesh might first move to a local market town, then to Chittagong (a regional city), then to Dhaka, and eventually perhaps to the Gulf or Europe. Each step builds economic resources, urban experience, and social contacts that make the next step possible. Stepped migration reduces the psychological and financial cost of each individual move and explains why major cities in LICs act as staging posts — or permanent destinations — for rural migrants who lack the resources or information for direct long-distance international movement.

Return migration: Many migrants eventually return to their origin — voluntarily (having achieved their economic goals, to retire, or because of homesickness and family ties) or involuntarily (deportation; conflict in the destination country). Return migration can bring significant benefits to origin communities — remittances accumulated during absence, skills acquired, and sometimes entrepreneurship. Example: Following the 2008 financial crisis, net migration between Poland and the UK reversed temporarily — Polish workers returned home as UK construction employment collapsed and the zloty strengthened against the pound. Return migration is a normal phase of many migration cycles, not an aberration.

Transnational migration: Contemporary migrants increasingly maintain active connections to both origin and destination rather than making a clean break. Transnational migrants send remittances regularly, make frequent return visits, maintain political engagement in both countries (sometimes voting in elections of both), and build businesses that straddle both. Cheap international phone calls, social media, and low-cost airlines have dramatically lowered the cost of maintaining transnational identities. Example: UK-based Somalis maintain close family connections and send significant remittances to Somalia — remittances to Somalia amount to ~$1.4bn/year (2023), exceeding total foreign aid to the country and equivalent to ~14% of GDP.

Patterns of Migration — Distance, Age, and Selectivity

Ravenstein's Laws of Migration (1885) — the foundation: Ernst Georg Ravenstein, analysing 19th-century UK census data, identified empirical regularities in migration patterns that remain broadly valid today: (1) most migrants move short distances; (2) migration proceeds step-by-step through the settlement hierarchy; (3) every migration stream produces a counter-stream; (4) long-distance migrants favour large cities; (5) urban residents migrate less than rural; (6) females migrate more than males over short distances; (7) migration increases with economic development. Ravenstein's laws were empirical observations, not theoretical explanations — but they established the systematic study of migration patterns.

Distance decay: Migration intensity decreases with increasing distance — the further the destination, the fewer migrants who reach it. This reflects the role of intervening obstacles: cost, uncertainty, and information all increase with distance. Most people who migrate at all make short moves: within the same region, to the nearest large city, or to a neighbouring country rather than overseas. Distance decay applies to internal migration (most UK internal migrants move within the same region); international migration (most Bangladeshi emigrants go to neighbouring India or to the Gulf, not to Europe or North America); and refugee flows (most Syrian refugees went to Turkey, Lebanon, and Jordan — neighbouring countries — not to Europe or North America, despite media focus on the latter).

Distance decay is modified by: Migration networks (chain migration creates long-distance flows that defy simple distance relationships — the Sylhet-Tower Hamlets corridor operates at 8,000 km because of network effects, not proximity); Transport costs (the dramatic fall in air travel costs since the 1970s has reduced the friction of distance); Colonial and linguistic ties (Algerians to France, Indians and Pakistanis to the UK, Brazilians to Portugal — historical connections create migration corridors that override simple distance relationships).

Age selectivity: Voluntary migration is strongly skewed toward young adults, typically aged 18–35. This age selectivity reflects several factors:

  • Lifetime earnings horizon: A 25-year-old migrating to a higher-wage economy captures the premium over a 40-year career; a 55-year-old captures it over only 10 years — the return on the costs and disruption of migration is much higher for the young.
  • Lower sunk costs: Younger people have fewer established career investments, less property ownership, fewer children in school, and weaker community ties — lower costs of leaving.
  • Physical adaptability: Long and physically demanding journeys (crossing the Sahara, the Aegean, or the English Channel) are more feasible for younger, healthier individuals.
  • Labour market recruitment: Employers and immigration systems recruit primarily for working-age labour — visa schemes typically favour those of working age.

Age selectivity creates characteristic demographic effects: source areas lose their most productive working-age cohort, leaving ageing, feminised populations with high dependency ratios (rural eastern Europe; Mexican sending communities); destination areas gain working-age bulges in their population pyramids (Gulf states have extreme male working-age surpluses; UK working-age cohorts are swelled by immigrant workers). Example: In Mirpur district, Pakistani-administered Kashmir — a major source of UK Pakistani migrants — the working-age male cohort is visibly depleted in local population data, with villages dominated by women, children, and elderly men while the male breadwinners are in Birmingham, Bradford, or Manchester.

Gender selectivity: Gender patterns in migration vary significantly by migration type and context. Historically, long-distance economic migration was male-dominated — men moved first as labour migrants, women followed later through family reunification. This pattern remains common in Gulf labour migration (construction, oil industry). Increasingly, however, female-dominated migration streams have emerged — Filipino, Indonesian, and Sri Lankan women as domestic workers in the Gulf and East Asia; Bangladeshi and Cambodian women as garment workers migrating to urban factories; Central American women migrating to the US as domestic and care workers. Female migrants are often more vulnerable to exploitation and abuse, particularly in domestic service roles with limited legal protection.

Skill selectivity — the brain drain: Immigration systems in HICs (the UK's points-based system, Canada's Express Entry, Australia's skills visa) actively recruit educated and skilled workers. This creates a brain drain from LICs — the emigration of the most educated and skilled people from countries that invested in their education but cannot offer competitive wages. Example: An estimated 50% of Ghanaian-trained doctors work overseas; the Philippines trains nurses explicitly for export (with ~130,000 Filipino nurses working in the USA alone); ~$50bn of Indian-origin PhDs work in US universities. The brain drain removes the human capital most needed for LIC development while simultaneously benefiting HIC economies — a significant dimension of global inequality in migration.

Constraints, obstacles, and barriers — integrating the patterns:

The patterns described above — distance decay, age selectivity, gender selectivity, skill selectivity — are all produced by the interaction of push and pull factors with intervening obstacles. The most important contemporary constraint on migration is the system of national borders and immigration controls that HICs have constructed since the late 20th century. In 1900, most international borders were largely open — a person could travel from India to the UK with no visa requirement (both were British imperial subjects). Today, an unskilled Nigerian seeking to migrate to the UK faces a near-absolute legal barrier, regardless of how large the wage differential is — the immigration system as intervening obstacle is more powerful than most push and pull factors combined.

This creates a fundamental asymmetry in global migration: capital flows freely across borders; goods flow with relatively low barriers (WTO rules); but labour — particularly low-skilled labour from LICs — faces enormous barriers that capital and goods do not. Economists generally agree that removing labour migration barriers would be one of the largest possible contributions to global poverty reduction (estimates suggest fully open borders would double global GDP). The fact that this does not happen reflects the political economy of migration — the benefits of labour mobility are diffuse (cheaper goods, more dynamism, recipient economies) while the perceived costs are concentrated (wage competition for low-skilled native workers, cultural change) and politically salient. This tension between economic logic and political reality is the central challenge of migration policy everywhere.

5.2 Internal Migration

Rural–Urban Migration — Causes

Rural–urban migration is the movement of people from rural areas to towns and cities. It is the dominant form of internal migration globally and the primary driver of urbanisation — the rising proportion of people living in urban areas. In 2007, for the first time in history, more than half the world's population lived in urban areas; by 2023, the proportion had reached ~57%, and the UN projects ~68% urban by 2050, almost entirely driven by internal rural-urban migration in LICs and MICs.

Push factors from rural areas (LIC/MIC context):

  • Agricultural unemployment and underemployment: Mechanisation of farming (tractors, combine harvesters, mechanical pumps) has displaced rural labour across Asia and Latin America. In India, the Green Revolution dramatically raised yields but reduced labour demand per hectare — displaced agricultural workers became the first wave of urban migrants. Seasonal underemployment is also critical: subsistence farmers may have work for only 4–6 months of the agricultural year, leaving a long idle season during which urban migration is economically rational.
  • Land fragmentation and landlessness: In South Asia and Sub-Saharan Africa, inheritance systems divide land among sons with each generation — farms become too small to support a family. Landless agricultural labourers (who own no land) have little to hold them in rural areas. In Bihar (India's poorest state and a major source of rural-urban migrants), ~60% of agricultural households are landless or near-landless.
  • Rural poverty and income gap: The urban-rural income differential is the core quantitative driver. In India, urban incomes are roughly 3–4× rural incomes on average. The Todaro model explains that even urban unemployment does not deter migration when this premium is large enough. In sub-Saharan Africa, urban-rural income ratios of 5:1 or higher are common in formal sector employment.
  • Declining rural services: Schools, hospitals, banks, and markets are progressively withdrawn from low-population rural areas in a 'vicious circle' — as young people leave, services become unviable, making remaining conditions less attractive, driving further emigration. Remote rural areas in the UK, eastern Europe, and parts of Latin America face this dynamic.
  • Environmental factors: Drought, soil exhaustion, desertification, and climate-related crop failures push people from the land. The Sahel belt — from Senegal to Somalia — has experienced repeated drought-driven out-migration. In Bangladesh, river bank erosion (chars) periodically destroys villages along the major rivers, displacing millions into urban areas.

Pull factors to urban areas (LIC/MIC context):

  • Higher wages and employment opportunities: Cities concentrate manufacturing, services, construction, and trade — all offering wages substantially above rural alternatives. Mumbai's textile industry, Dhaka's garment factories, and Lagos's informal economy all attract rural migrants seeking income improvement.
  • Access to services: Hospitals, universities, secondary schools, banks, and government services are concentrated in cities, making urban residence attractive for families seeking healthcare and education for children — not just income.
  • Social mobility: Cities offer greater anonymity — freedom from restrictive caste, class, and gender expectations of village communities — and greater opportunity for individual social advancement. For Dalit (lower-caste) Indians or women from conservative rural communities, cities can represent social as well as economic opportunity.
  • Established migrant networks (chain migration): The presence of friends, family, and community members from the same origin village dramatically reduces the perceived risk and cost of moving. Networks provide accommodation on arrival, help finding work, and social support. Chain migration is the process by which early pioneer migrants reduce the intervening obstacles for subsequent migrants from the same origin — creating self-sustaining migration streams.
Case study — Rural-urban migration to Mumbai and Dharavi

Scale and context: India has an estimated 600 million internal migrants — the largest internal migration flow of any country. Rural-urban migration is driven by agricultural mechanisation and the Green Revolution's labour displacement, growing rural-urban income differentials (urban formal sector wages ~3–4× rural agricultural wages), and India's rapid industrial and service sector growth. Mumbai (population ~21 million) is the primary destination for migrants from Maharashtra, Uttar Pradesh, Bihar, and Rajasthan.

Dharavi: Located on 2.1 km² of prime land in central Mumbai between the Western and Central railway lines, Dharavi is Asia's largest informal settlement with an estimated population of 600,000–1 million (official census figures severely undercount). Originally a coastal fishing village, it expanded rapidly from the 1880s as tannery workers (Khatik community from Tamil Nadu), potters (Kumbhar from Gujarat), and later textile and garment workers settled. Today Dharavi contains a dense, productive informal economy — leather goods, recycling (including ~80% of Mumbai's plastics recycling), garments, pottery, food processing — generating an estimated $650m–$1bn/year in economic output.

Push factors driving migration to Dharavi: Rural poverty and unemployment (especially drought-prone Maharashtra, and agricultural underemployment in UP and Bihar); Green Revolution mechanisation reducing farm labour demand; landlessness in Bihar (~60% near-landless agricultural households); caste discrimination limiting rural economic opportunities for Dalits.

Pull factors: Proximity to Mumbai's enormous formal and informal economy; established community networks from specific sending villages (chain migration — particular streets in Dharavi are dominated by migrants from specific districts); affordable informal housing (rental rooms from ~₹2,000/month); access to the informal sector as an entry point.

Stepped migration: Many Dharavi residents followed a stepped path — first moving from village to local market town (for seasonal work or small trading), then to a regional city (Nashik, Nagpur, Patna), before making the final move to Mumbai. This reduces the shock of direct rural-to-megacity migration, builds urban experience and contacts, and allows economic accumulation at each step that funds the next.

Impacts of Rural–Urban Migration on Source and Destination Areas

Impacts on source (rural) areas:

  • Remittances: Money sent home by urban migrants is often the single largest income source for rural households in LICs. Globally, remittances to LICs exceed $500bn/year — more than total foreign aid. In Bihar, remittances from urban migrants (primarily in Mumbai, Delhi, and Punjab) are a critical part of household survival strategies for landless and small-farming families. Remittances can fund housing improvements, school fees, and health costs — but can also create dependency and inflate local prices.
  • Population structure change: Rural-urban migration is strongly age- and gender-selective. Young men (18–35) migrate first, leaving a feminised, ageing rural population. Source area population pyramids develop a pronounced deficit in the 20–40 male age cohort. This creates a high dependency ratio in rural areas (few working-age adults supporting many children and elderly), reduces agricultural labour availability, and increases the burden on women who remain (managing farms and households alone). Example: In Bihar's major sending districts (Darbhanga, Madhubani, Sitamarhi), the sex ratio in the 20–40 age group in villages is typically heavily female — male migration is visible in census data as a 'missing cohort'.
  • Loss of human capital: The most educated, most ambitious, and most economically productive young people tend to migrate — a 'rural brain drain' that can reduce the human capital and entrepreneurial capacity of source communities. However, this is partially offset by remittances and by return migration, where migrants return with urban skills and capital.
  • Agricultural labour shortage: In some source areas where migration has been substantial, a shortage of agricultural labour has driven up rural wages (benefiting those who remain) or forced a shift toward more mechanised or less labour-intensive farming. In parts of coastal Andhra Pradesh, the outflow of young male labour to Gulf construction has raised agricultural wages for remaining workers.
  • Return migration benefits: Migrants who return (seasonally, temporarily, or permanently) bring back income, urban skills, business ideas, and social remittances (new attitudes toward gender, education, and family size). Return migration can catalyse entrepreneurship and development in source areas — if the economic environment is supportive.

Impacts on destination (urban) areas:

  • Labour supply: Rural-urban migrants provide the workforce for construction, manufacturing, domestic service, transport, and the informal economy — essential to urban economic growth. Mumbai's economy depends critically on its migrant labour pool; without migration, India's urbanisation and economic growth would stall.
  • Population structure change: Urban receiving areas gain a large young adult working-age cohort, which lowers the dependency ratio (more workers per dependant) and may contribute to a 'demographic dividend'. However, the male-skewed age-sex structure of many migrant inflows creates social challenges — as in Gulf cities, where male migrant workers vastly outnumber women and local residents.
  • Informal settlements (slums): When urban housing supply cannot keep pace with migration-driven population growth, informal settlements expand on the urban periphery or in interstitial spaces (like Dharavi between railway lines). Informal settlements are characterised by: insecure tenure (residents do not legally own their plots); inadequate services (water, sanitation, electricity — though Dharavi now has partial electrification and piped water); high population density; and mixed residential-industrial land use. In Mumbai, ~60% of the population lives in informal settlements occupying only ~8% of the land area — an acute spatial inequality.
  • Pressure on services: Rapid population growth through in-migration strains urban infrastructure — water supply, sewage, roads, schools, and hospitals. Mumbai's water system was designed for a much smaller city; the addition of millions of informal settlement residents has created chronic water shortages in peripheral areas.
  • Cultural diversity and social tensions: Migrants bring regional languages, cultural practices, and food traditions that enrich urban culture but can also create social tensions, particularly if migrants are perceived to compete for scarce jobs and housing with established urban residents. In Mumbai, the Shiv Sena political party rose to power partly on anti-migrant sentiment (particularly against migrants from UP and Bihar), advocating 'Maharashtra for Maharashtrians'.
Dharavi — impacts in detail

Impacts on source areas (Bihar/UP villages): Remittances from Dharavi workers are the primary cash income for many village households — funding food purchases in lean seasons, school fees, medical emergencies, and modest housing improvements. The male working-age cohort is visibly depleted in sending villages; women manage farms and households. Agricultural labour availability has declined; some farms have been consolidated or converted to less labour-intensive uses.

Impacts on Dharavi/Mumbai: Dharavi contributes significantly to Mumbai's economy through its informal industrial production. However, residents face: acute overcrowding (estimated 280,000 people per km² — among the world's highest densities); water scarcity (water supply typically 2–4 hours/day); inadequate sanitation (one public toilet per ~1,440 people historically, though improving through SRA schemes); high disease risk (tuberculosis rates very high; COVID-19 hit Dharavi severely in 2020 before a community-led containment effort attracted global attention); and insecure tenure (threat of eviction for redevelopment — see the Dharavi Redevelopment Project, awarded to Adani group in 2022, which has displaced thousands during the planning process).

Urban–Rural Migration — Counter-Urbanisation

Counter-urbanisation is the movement of people from urban areas to smaller towns, villages, and rural areas — the partial reversal of urbanisation. It is characteristic of post-industrial HICs where urban disamenities push people out of cities and improved transport and communications reduce the necessity of urban residence. Counter-urbanisation was first identified in the USA in the 1970s and became a significant trend in the UK from the 1960s–70s onwards.

Causes of counter-urbanisation:

  • Urban disamenities: High housing costs, crime, congestion, air pollution, noise, and overcrowding push people out of large cities — particularly families with children seeking space and perceived safety. London's property prices (median house price ~£530,000 in 2023) are prohibitive for first-time buyers and young families, driving out-migration to smaller cities and rural areas.
  • Improved transport: High-speed rail and motorway networks have made commuting from rural and small-town areas viable. The Cambridge–London King's Cross journey takes ~50 minutes; Bristol–London Paddington ~1h 45m — making daily or several-times-weekly commuting realistic.
  • Remote working (post-COVID acceleration): The pandemic-driven shift to home working removed the requirement for daily urban presence for many professional and office workers.
